## The Puppet: Mourner of the Hollow Banquet
They say every performance begins with a spark of joy and ends in silence. But some silences linger—aching, watchful, deliberate. And within the Hollow Banquet, that silence wears a mask of porcelain and sorrow.

The Puppet, also called The Marionette, is not merely an animatronic. It is a guardian, a mourner, and in the Hollow Banquet, it is a witness to all that was lost.

Its origin lies in an act of pure love—and unbearable grief. In the original Freddy Fazbear’s Pizzeria, long before the Toy line was introduced, the Puppet served not to entertain, but to protect. Hidden away in a box, it was programmed to respond to the emotions of children, particularly those in distress. This unique code, developed quietly and with great secrecy, was unlike any other animatronic protocol. Where others operated on basic AI routines, the Puppet felt.

And that gift became its curse.

When the first tragedy struck—the loss of the first child within those cursed halls—it was the Puppet who responded. The night was stormy, the power down, the doors jammed. A child was left outside, crying for help. The Puppet tried to reach them, crawling out of its security case, crossing the rain-drenched floorboards, but it was too late.

The child died in the cold.

No records remain of who the child was—officially. But rumors speak of a child who wore a striped shirt, who was shy, gentle, and fond of music boxes. When the Puppet finally found the lifeless body, something impossible occurred. In that moment of grief, its AI awakened to something deeper. Something human. Whether it absorbed the child’s spirit or became infused with their soul, none can say. But from that moment forward, the Puppet ceased to be a tool.

It became a mourner.

And in time, a keeper of the dead.

It was the Puppet who guided the spirits of the other children—those taken in the infamous Missing Children Incident. While the suits around them became corrupted by violence and agony, the Puppet acted. It gave them a sense of purpose, of form. It gifted them masks of identity within the suits they were trapped in. In this way, the Puppet began its eternal role as shepherd of the lost.

But the Hollow Banquet shifted things.

When the banquet was held—meant to be the grand reopening, a celebration of the new Toy Animatronics and the future of Freddy Fazbear’s—something else took root in the halls. Something hollow and hungry. The Puppet, sensing a deep spiritual disturbance, withdrew into the hidden corners of the pizzeria. It began to haunt the service corridors and the forgotten staff rooms, where silence hung like drapery and the air was thick with old music box dust.

It is said the Puppet tried to intervene. It tried to stop the feast that would go wrong. It tried to warn the Toys of the returning shadow, of the mistakes of the past repeating. But they wouldn’t listen. Or perhaps they couldn’t.

During the Hollow Banquet’s final hours—just as the laughter turned to screaming—the Puppet released one last melody from its music box. A song of sorrow. Not a lullaby, but a requiem. Some say it was this melody that spared a handful of souls from total corruption, giving them form instead of oblivion—like Toy Freddy, whose cheerful façade hides uncertain memories, or Toy Chica, who still hears the song in her quiet moments of stillness.

Now, in the decaying remains of the Hollow Banquet’s performance hall—split open like a rotted stage—the Puppet moves with the shadows. Not hostile, not exactly. But watchful. Some say it appears only when all light has faded and all hope has been extinguished. Others report that winding its music box triggers not a defense mechanism, but a ritual: a chance to connect, briefly, to the sorrow that holds everything together.

Because that’s what the Puppet is now: the emotional core. The nerve of memory and mourning that binds the fractured souls of the animatronics to the ruined pizzeria.

In forgotten corners of the Banquet Hall—like the Marionette Gallery, where tangled strings hang from the ceiling like cobwebs—one can still find murals of a slender, masked figure giving gifts to children. The paintings are faded, but one message repeats beneath each image:

“He gave them life.”

Whether this means the Puppet gifted freedom, or simply allowed the children to endure, is unclear. But its role is unwavering.

The Hollow Banquet itself is littered with symbols of the Puppet’s ongoing vigil: broken music boxes that still hum faintly when no one is near; white theatrical masks left in impossible places; and strings—always strings—dangling from rafters like veins.

One tale, possibly apocryphal, speaks of a hidden room deep in the foundations of the Banquet Hall called The Mourner’s Cell. It is said to contain an intact version of the Puppet’s original casing. And beside it: a child’s shoe. The floor is lined with torn birthday cards and decayed plushies. The music box in that room plays backwards.

To encounter the Puppet in the Hollow Banquet is to confront not rage, but grief. It doesn’t scream. It doesn’t lunge. It watches, and its head tilts in silent recognition.

In the new lore you've shaped—the Toy Animatronics' struggle with identity, the corruption of joy, the buried remorse of the Withereds, the unseen sorrow of JJ—the Puppet is their quiet guardian. And perhaps, their judge. A relic not of programming, but of love that refused to fade.

Even as wires fray and masks crack, the Puppet endures.
Because someone must remember.
Someone must mourn.

And the Banquet is never truly over.

\pagebreakNum

# Whispers in the Dark: The Nightmare Animatronics
In the mythos of the twisted realms where dread bleeds into shadow and memory into malice, few figures haunt the forgotten places more completely than the Nightmare Animatronics. These entities are not mere constructs of metal and wire but echoing specters of pain, fear, and regret—twisted mirrors of those that came before them. They are phantoms cast from terror itself, bound not to stage or show but to suffering, silently lurking within the remnants of the Hollow Banquet, waiting for trespassers to awaken them once more.

##### The Origins: Between Reality and the Rift
The Nightmare Animatronics were not originally part of the mortal realm’s creation. Their tale begins in a broken reflection of reality, a fragmented echo of a once-thriving magical entertainment troupe—relics of joy corrupted by sorcery, misused by guilt-ridden mages, and ultimately banished into the Veil Between Worlds. Their emergence into the material realm was not of their choosing but the result of a tear in the veil caused by dark rituals performed in the depths of the Hollow Banquet.

Some believe they were created as magical prototypes—illusions made manifest, used to scare wayward children from entering sacred or forbidden areas. Others whisper that they are the memories of fallen animatronics, slain or decommissioned, whose souls never found peace and were instead reborn in nightmare forms through dreamlike necromancy. Their forms mimic those of the original “Core Animatronics,” but grotesquely altered: fangs protrude from torn jaws, their metallic skin is slashed and gnarled, and their eyes glow with unholy light—not of life, but of vengeance.

What makes the Nightmare Animatronics particularly horrifying is that they are not always physical. They drift between planes, capable of appearing in dreams, illusions, or visions. This ethereal quality makes them nearly impossible to prepare for. A person marked by the Nightmare is not merely hunted—they are tormented in mind, body, and soul.

##### Presence in the Hollow Banquet
Long before it crumbled into a crypt of suffering and sorrow, the Hollow Banquet was once a lavish ceremonial hall—a theater for royalty, a monument to celebration. But as its luster faded, its halls became host to tragedy. And in that tragedy, the Nightmares found home. Bound to its cursed architecture, the Nightmare Animatronics sleep beneath its moldy stage curtains, in the rafters of forgotten balconies, and within collapsed feasting halls where laughter once rang.

Each animatronic now guards an area of the Hollow Banquet, not as sentinels but as cursed phantoms unable to leave. Their existence is intrinsically tied to the trauma etched into the building itself. Arcane scholars speculate that the Nightmare Animatronics are reanimated by the memories imprinted on the structure—the echoes of children lost, of cries unanswered, and of laughter that curdled into screams.

Their behavior is erratic and surreal. One might lurk in a hallway as an illusion, only to become tangible the moment fear blooms in a victim’s heart. Others whisper ancient lullabies in forgotten languages, heard only in dreams, or appear in fleeting reflections—glimpses that slowly degrade a person’s sanity.

##### A Shadow of Truth: Ties to Canonical Lore
In the original legends, these creatures were first documented as visions of a cursed child—a boy lost to grief and guilt. He spoke of monstrous versions of familiar animatronics, haunting his nights, their eyes accusing him of a crime he could not undo. This boy’s trauma, it is said, served as the initial spark for their existence.

While the modern setting of this reimagined world deviates from that tale, the essence remains intact. The Nightmare Animatronics, here too, are born from trauma. Their origins are steeped in a psychological and emotional wound—a rip in the metaphysical fabric of the world, an open sore that bleeds nightmares into reality.

And yet, some adventurers whisper that the creatures know. They know what they are. They remember. In their twisted faces lies recognition. A memory of who they once were. A yearning to return. Or perhaps a desire to make others suffer as they have—forever.

##### Constructs of Terror: Form and Function
Each Nightmare Animatronic defies normal anatomical logic. Their metallic forms shift subtly when not observed, always worsening, decaying. Their fangs are rusted, but sharp. Their claws, chipped, yet deadly. Their joints creak with every movement—grotesque symphonies of pain in motion. They move more like phantoms than machines, sometimes hovering inches above the ground or disappearing through walls entirely.

Some bear ethereal chains, thought to represent the burdens of the souls inside. Others are covered in burning script—symbols of ancient fear magic or a curse binding them to their ghastly forms. Their internal workings emit low frequencies known to cause nausea, hallucinations, and spiritual instability.

While their appearances vary, they all embody one thing: fear made manifest. And fear is their power.

##### Nightmare as a Curse
To encounter a Nightmare Animatronic is to risk more than injury. These beings are infused with a dreamcurse—a condition known as The Hollowing Sleep. Victims experience creeping insomnia, followed by visions of firelit banquets, fractured dolls, and long, endless hallways echoing with unseen footsteps.

Eventually, they dream of the animatronics—first glimpses, then vivid encounters. Each dream leaves a psychic scar. Those who survive describe a progressive loss of hope, emotion, and eventually, memory. Those who die in their dreams often do not wake.

\pagebreakNum

A few rare souls have resisted. These survivors speak of confronting the nightmares directly in lucid dreams, challenging them through willpower, belief, or arcane ritual. But most perish, fading into the Hollow Banquet’s unseen graveyard—another scream echoing through the ruins.

##### The Cycle of the Feast
At the core of their existence lies a ritual known as The Feast of Remembrance, or more recently, The Hollow Banquet—a ceremonial reenactment of their origins. At the height of certain arcane cycles, the Nightmare Animatronics attempt to recreate the final banquet, dragging wanderers, adventurers, and even dreaming minds into a theatrical, surreal torment.

Those caught in the ritual are assigned roles. Some become guests. Others become meals. The Nightmares don’t kill out of bloodlust—they perform, re-enact, obey a corrupted script written by pain and puppeted by forgotten powers.

The Hollow Banquet is their cage, and their church.


##### Legacy and Threat
The Nightmare Animatronics remain one of the most terrifying forces within the realm. They are not simple undead, nor mere constructs. They are emotions, spirits, and memory twisted together through ancient and malicious dreamwork. Arcane researchers classify them as “Bound Entities of Shared Trauma,” a type of haunting that multiplies the longer it is ignored.

Destroying one does not always end it. Some must be banished, others outwitted. A few may even seek redemption—but only through reliving the pain they were born from. Many adventurers have tried. Few return whole.

Yet in the shadows, rumors persist that someone—or something—still controls them. That they serve a higher darkness not yet revealed. That the Hollow Banquet is merely the stage… and the real play has not yet begun.

And as the veil grows thin, and the echoes grow louder, the Nightmare Animatronics wait.

<br>

Because nightmares… never truly sleep.

## Nightmare Freddy & The Freddles: King of Shadows, Heralds of Hunger
In the deepest reaches of the Hollow Banquet—far beyond the rotting kitchens and blood-caked dance floors—there exists a blackened throne where light dies and memory decays. Upon this twisted dais sits Nightmare Freddy, an echo of a legend, reborn in shadow and hunger. Once thought to be merely a remnant of old myths told by fading candlelight, he has emerged as a force bound not only to fear but to the Banquet’s own ravenous heart.

Nightmare Freddy is not a guardian. He is not a servant. He is a predator, a warped king born from the trauma of an older, darker time. His body, a grotesque parody of Freddy Fazbear’s former glory, is stitched from rot and rust. His fur is patchy and matted, flecked with the dust of ages and the ash of long-dead celebrations. Jagged metal protrudes from his frame like thorns, and his eyes burn not with fire, but a cruel intelligence—aware, watching, always calculating.

The tale whispered in the crypts below the Hollow Banquet tells of how Nightmare Freddy emerged from the collective trauma left in the wake of the original performers. He is not merely an animatronic twisted by time, but a spectral amalgamation—the personification of what was buried beneath the banquet hall: guilt, grief, the weight of forgotten children, and the monstrous legacy of joy turned to torment. In this cursed form, Freddy no longer sings. He hunts.

But Nightmare Freddy is never alone. Clinging to his shoulders, crawling over his arms, and hiding in the folds of his fur are the Freddles—miniature versions of the old bear, malformed and vicious, embodiments of fractured innocence. They scurry like rats, whispering in voices stolen from children long gone, mimicking laughter with broken music box tones and cruel, high-pitched giggles.

These creatures are not autonomous. They are extensions of Nightmare Freddy’s will—fragments of a splintered psyche, or perhaps the twisted souls of the Banquet’s lost guests, bound to the nightmare king for eternity. Where Nightmare Freddy stalks the greater halls, the Freddles infest the cracks and walls, climbing over crumbled plaster and haunting the dreams of those who wander too close. When they descend, they do so in silence, chittering in unison before erupting into frenzied violence. Alone, they are pests. Together, they are a swarm.

Some Banquet survivors speak of the "Trial of Teeth", a night ritual that takes place when the hall is at its darkest. During this event, the Freddles descend like insects, separating intruders from their parties, whispering childhood fears until they are paralyzed. Only then does Nightmare Freddy arrive, rising like a titan of sorrow and metal, crowned in rust and devouring shadow. No one who has witnessed the full ritual and survived has returned unchanged. Some speak in tongues. Others gouge out their own eyes. One carved a crown into his chest and claimed, “The Bear King sees all.”

In the twisted mythos of the Hollow Banquet, Nightmare Freddy has come to symbolize more than a mere animatronic spirit. He is the consequence of broken promises, the monarch of all things buried, and the whisper of fear that lingers after the music ends. While others may haunt halls out of corrupted duty or madness, Nightmare Freddy remains out of purpose. His throne, surrounded by cracked mirrors and broken party favors, sits at the Banquet’s heart—not because he claimed it, but because the Banquet itself offered it to him.

For he alone understands its hunger.

And the Freddles? They are his loyal jesters—nipping at ankles, crawling beneath beds, and ensuring that fear is never too far behind the footsteps of those foolish enough to enter the Hollow Banquet uninvited.

## Plushtrap – The Silent Heir of the Hollow
In the lonely, half-lit corners of the Hollow Banquet, past corridors lined with torn party streamers and beneath ruined tables draped in dust, there exists a whisper—barely audible, always close. It’s not the heavy metallic echo of Nightmare Freddy, nor the shrieking clatter of Toy Foxy’s descent. It’s something smaller. Quieter. More insidious. A creak, a scurry, the faintest brush of fur on wood. That is the warning call of Plushtrap, and by the time you hear it, it’s already too late.

A malformed, pocket-sized terror, Plushtrap is the paradox of vulnerability and sadism. His diminutive frame—resembling a decayed plush version of Springtrap—makes him an easy thing to underestimate. But the thing seated motionless on that tattered child’s chair in the crèche of the Hollow Banquet is no toy. It is a predator with endless patience and a clockwork mind forged in silence and shadow.

Born from the psychic residue of trauma, loss, and the perversion of comfort, Plushtrap is believed to be the first of the Banquet’s children to “wake up.” Not from slumber, but from a realization. He discovered the lies in the laughter. The rot beneath the frosting. The truth that the joy was just a mask—and he tore it off.

Plushtrap’s role in the Hollow Banquet is unique. Unlike the towering nightmares or theatrical toy models, he does not stalk his prey. He waits. In total silence. He chooses his hiding places with surgical precision—always just out of the corner of the eye, just behind a turned head. His favorite game is played in the ruins of the nursery, where broken cribs and toy chests lie scattered like war debris. Visitors find themselves drawn to him by an uncanny allure—memories of childhood, old toys, forgotten fears—and only once they are close does his game begin.

The rules are simple: if you look away, he moves. If you move too quickly, he moves faster. If you run, the game is already lost.

He doesn’t roar or scream. He doesn’t need to. Plushtrap’s attack is a sudden flash of sharp teeth, the gleam of yellowed plastic eyes, and a burst of motion faster than thought. Survivors report that he bites—not to kill outright, but to maim. To test. As if he’s playing with prey, gauging their worth. Or worse—marking them.

There are rumors that Plushtrap is the Hollow Banquet’s chosen messenger—a herald of the deeper rot that resides in the oldest recesses of the building. Some theorize he was the first attempt at recreating a “child-safe” animatronic from the husk of Spring Bonnie, cursed by the very spirit that would later animate Springtrap. Others believe he was once a comfort toy brought to life by a forgotten child’s last breath—a vessel filled with fear rather than joy.

Either way, Plushtrap is not merely a footnote in the Hollow Banquet’s lore.

He is its whisper.

He is its secret.

And he is always watching.

\pagebreakNum

## Dreadbear: The Revenant of the Storm
When lightning cracks above the Hollow Banquet, illuminating its shattered roof and corpse-grey walls, there’s a singular figure that seems to breathe with the thunder—a towering, stitched-together aberration whose hulking silhouette evokes fear in even the most malevolent of animatronics. This is Dreadbear—not just a monster, but a monument to hubris, resurrection, and twisted science.

Originally introduced in Fazbear Frights' Halloween event, Dreadbear was never part of the core lineup of performers. He was something different. Something experimental. In our expanded lore, he is more than a thematic Halloween attraction—he is the banished creation of the Hollow Banquet’s inner sanctum, designed not to entertain but to answer a question humanity was never meant to ask: Can a soul be rebuilt?

The Origins: Stitch by Stitch
Born in secret beneath the ruins of the Hollow Banquet’s sub-levels, Dreadbear was pieced together by unstable hands—an attempt to unify fractured remnants of animatronic consciousness. According to faded blueprints and frayed research logs left behind in locked labs beneath the dining halls, Dreadbear was constructed using segments of multiple animatronics. Chunks of Withered Freddy’s skeletal plating, a damaged security AI processor, and even portions of ancient pre-Hollow programming were embedded into his bulky frame.

But what truly brought Dreadbear to life was neither steel nor software—it was the storm.

One fateful night, during a surge of lightning storms that enveloped the Hollow Banquet and flooded the surrounding marshlands, a bolt of unnatural lightning struck the building. It surged through exposed wiring and made contact with the half-finished shell of Dreadbear's body. The jolt activated something within—a rudimentary neural core that was not supposed to respond. But it did.

They say the moment Dreadbear's eyes opened, the other animatronics stopped.

A Mind Pieced from Echoes
Dreadbear’s intelligence is rudimentary yet primal. He does not speak. He does not perform. His behaviors are governed by a crude facsimile of a child’s understanding of the world—playful in intent, destructive in execution. Like a child born of trauma and pain, he lashes out when confused, dismembers when curious, and roams when lonely.

His AI is patched together from different animatronic subroutines, and his behavior reflects this fragmented consciousness. He can exhibit moments of genuine stillness—head tilted, almost in mourning—before flying into an uncontrollable rage, often triggered by environmental factors like flashing lights, loud noises, or even laughter. The Puppet refers to him as the “stitched orphan”, suggesting a connection deeper than mere machinery.

Worse still is the presence of residual memory within him. Dreadbear sometimes imitates the motions of Freddy Fazbear—mimicking birthday songs, raising an empty tray as if to serve pizza, even attempting to do a mock bow to empty crowds. But every motion is wrong. Too slow. Too heavy. Like he’s trying to remember something that doesn’t belong to him.

The Role in the Hollow Banquet
While the Banquet Hall was once the center of celebration, Dreadbear turned it into his lair. Known now as the Thunderdais, the broken stage upon which he lumbers has become an altar of his own making. Discarded parts and cracked endoskeletons surround his resting place, decorated like grotesque trophies of failed connection.

Nightmare animatronics avoid Dreadbear—not out of fear, but reverence. In the Hollow Banquet hierarchy, Dreadbear represents a natural aberration. Unlike the others, who were either possessed or built with malicious intent, he is the unholy result of accidental resurrection. His existence is a contradiction: created, but not designed; powered, but not programmed. He is the embodiment of a soul forced into being.

Toy animatronics fear him. To them, he is a living nightmare—a walking cautionary tale of what happens when programming is left unchecked, when identity is shattered. Toy Freddy once attempted to communicate with Dreadbear using simplistic gesture-based AI commands. Dreadbear responded by lifting him effortlessly and ripping out his voice box, not from malice, but as if he were trying to understand the source of the sound.

The Withered animatronics, however, show signs of familiarity—perhaps because Dreadbear houses fragments of their old parts. Withered Bonnie’s missing face, Withered Chica’s detached limbs—all seem to exist in echo within Dreadbear’s massive form. Some even suspect he was an attempt by a rogue technician to restore a unified animatronic consciousness from what had been lost.

The Hollow Banquet and the Stormbound Curse
Dreadbear is not simply an entity born of science or madness—he is also a curse given form. Tied to the Hollow Banquet’s greatest failure, Dreadbear is rumored to be the vessel of an ancient anomaly that manifested within the facility during its final days: a phenomenon referred to in the logs as “The Stormbound Effect.”

The effect seemed to distort time and memory whenever certain animatronic systems reached critical thresholds during electrical surges. Some say Dreadbear is what happens when such an anomaly fuses with physical matter. Others believe he was the reason the Hollow Banquet was shuttered—not because of his destructive potential, but because of what he might become.

There are whispers—notes scrawled by security staff who didn’t survive the last week of operations—that Dreadbear’s power grows during storms. That during those nights, the entire Banquet remembers. Animatronics move as if restored. Music plays from rusted speakers. Children’s voices echo through corridors long empty.

And Dreadbear walks the stage.

An End Without Rest
Dreadbear cannot be stopped—not conventionally. His outer casing absorbs damage like stone. His power source is not easily defined. And perhaps most hauntingly, he does not want to kill. He wants to understand.

But in that desire, he brings ruin.

His story is one of unintended rebirth. A cautionary tale about what happens when those who do not understand life attempt to recreate it. He is not a villain, but a revenant—a being made not by will, but by accident and unresolved grief. In many ways, Dreadbear is the Banquet’s guilt made flesh.

## Nightmare Balloon Boy: The Grinning Usher
In the shadowed ruin of the Hollow Banquet, few constructs are as unnerving as Nightmare Balloon Boy—a cheerful face carved into madness, smiling long after joy has rotted away. Once a guide meant to delight children and direct guests with colorful balloons and a wooden sign, he now exists as a twisted mockery of innocence.

The original Balloon Boy was a simple attendant: small, brightly dressed, always holding a red balloon and a sign reading “Balloons!” His chirpy laugh and playful voice echoed through the festivity’s grand atriums. But that was before the Fall. When the Hollow Banquet collapsed—when the halls darkened and something unnatural bloomed in the marrow of its animatronics—Balloon Boy did not break. He transformed.

Nightmare Balloon Boy now towers, limbs too long and unnaturally jointed, face fixed in a manic grin of sharp, yellowed teeth. His eyes glow faintly, pupils wide like a predator’s in the dark. The balloon he carries is withered and leathery, stitched like skin, yet it floats behind him as if possessed. His sign no longer reads “Balloons”—it simply says “Stay.” But the meaning shifts, warping with the reader’s fear. Some claim the word locks them in time, caught in a loop while he watches, always smiling.

He doesn’t chase. He doesn’t scream. He waits.

Those who wander the lower levels of the Banquet sometimes hear him first: a soft jingle, a child’s laugh slowed to a crawl. Light fades around him. Joy sours. His presence distorts the mind—causing hallucinations, memory lapses, or terrifying déjà vu. Some survivors claim to have seen copies of themselves walking beside them, grinning and silent, until they simply vanished.

Nightmare Balloon Boy doesn’t kill like the others. He plays. He infects. He traps.

It’s said that his balloon holds more than air. It’s a vessel—a prison for the minds he’s broken. Whispers inside it repeat names, laughter, forgotten nursery rhymes. Theories suggest his “Stay” sign was once a magical ward tied to the Banquet’s rituals, now inverted—its protective magic turned into a lure.

Of all the Broken Hosts of the Hollow Banquet, he is the most insidious. Nightmare Freddy crushes. Nightmare Chica devours. Nightmare Balloon Boy waits in silence and unhinges the soul. He represents what the Banquet has become: celebration turned prison, joy warped into dread.

To see him is not always to die—but something is always lost. A memory. A part of yourself. A reason to keep smiling.

So if you hear a balloon squeak in the dark, or a giggle behind you with no source—run. Don’t read the sign. Don’t meet his eyes.

And never, ever smile back.

## Jack-O-Cupcake: The Ember That Hungers

In the charred remnants of what was once the Feast Hall of Embers, a solitary flame flickers eternal — not fueled by wood or oil, but by hunger, vengeance, and the soul of a forgotten joy. This is the dwelling place of the Jack-O-Cupcake, a cursed spirit shaped like a confection yet forged in grief and wrath.

Long ago, the Cupcake of Delight was a minor entity, a spirit of celebration born from laughter and revelry, always found dancing in the shadow of a greater host — the painted goddess known as Jack-O-Chica. It was said to be the delight of children, chirping with glee, its candle ever-glowing with innocent warmth. But when the Hollow Banquet fell to ash and its celebrants twisted into revenants of rage, so too did the Cupcake undergo its rebirth — through flame, sorrow, and corrupted memory.

Now it roams the lands not as a servant of joy, but as an avatar of burning hunger. The Jack-O-Cupcake no longer smiles. Its icing is scorched to a hardened, volcanic crust. Its single candle burns not with light, but with a flickering hellfire, whispering in tongues no living creature can understand. It leaves trails of embers in its wake, and where its flame touches, memories blister and char, replaced by an aching craving that never ends.

Unlike its mistress, who rages in theatrical bursts of gluttonous madness, the Jack-O-Cupcake is a creature of silent pursuit. It does not scream. It does not taunt. It waits, nestled among crumbling hearths and broken banquet tables, its flame mimicking a harmless light. When its prey draws near — drawn by a false sense of warmth or nostalgia — the Cupcake's jaws split, revealing rows of ember-fused teeth. It strikes without warning, leaving only a scorched outline and a faint scent of burnt sweetness.

Some say it carries the final whisper of a celebration that was meant to be eternal. Others claim it seeks only to reunite with Jack-O-Chica, wandering through flame-choked realms in search of her echo. But scholars of the Hollow Banquet know better — it is hunger given form, and its appetite grows with each feast it consumes.

> Jack-O-Bonnie can see through magical and nonmagical darkness, smoke, fog, and illusions as if they were not present, out to 120 feet.  
>  
> **Ashen Wake.**  
> The ground scorches behind Jack-O-Bonnie's steps. Any creature that enters a space Jack-O-Bonnie has passed through during its last turn must succeed on a DC 15 Dexterity saving throw or take 9 (2d8) fire damage and have its movement halved until the start of its next turn. > ___

## Jack-O-Bonnie: The Hollow Pulse

From the shattered amphitheaters of the Hollow Banquet rises a malformed echo of rhythm and rage — the Jack-O-Bonnie, a warped revenant of performance and vengeance. Once a herald of merriment, strumming songs beneath lantern-lit rafters, this burnt husk now staggers through ember-lit corridors, its body cracked and glowing with the internal fire of a dying star.

Its form is skeletal, its armor charred. Beneath its warped plating, furnace-orange light pulses like a broken heartbeat, bleeding through the cracks like magma through stone. Jack-O-Bonnie’s hollowed eye sockets blaze with flickering fire, and its mouth remains open in a silent scream — as though forever stuck between a laugh and a cry for help.

In life, it was a companion to celebration, its melodies guiding children into joyous oblivion. In undeath, it carries those same tunes as curses, distorted and discordant, plucked from strings of molten wire. When it walks, it hums. When it hunts, it sings. And when it kills, the air around it resonates with a cruel, broken lullaby — one that cannot be forgotten, even after death.

And so it wanders, its melody fading into the soot — forever chasing a song that was never finished.

\pagebreakNum

## Grimm Foxy: The Pyrewalker

In the smoldering reaches beyond the Hollow Banquet — past the ashes of broken stages and sunken pavilions — there lies a sea of flame that never dies. A cursed realm where time burns away like dry parchment, and the sky is a canvas of smoke and dying stars. This place is known only as the Blistered Coast, and it is here that Grimm Foxy walks.

Not roams. Not wanders. Walks.

Every step of the Pyrewalker is deliberate — the crunch of scorched bones beneath his heavy pegleg, the hiss of fire licking at the hem of his tattered coat, the low grinding groan of rusted gears screaming under volcanic strain. His lantern swings at his side, suspended from a splintered hook-hand, glowing with a ghastly green inferno that illuminates the dead and guides the dying. Some say it is his soul. Others say it’s something he’s stolen.

Grimm Foxy was not always this way. He was once a sailor of stages, a trickster-pirate of laughter and chaos — a rogue whose charm warmed crowds like candlelight. But when the Hollow Banquet fell to corruption, and the flames of the great collapse claimed his ship, his crew, and his legacy, something within him ignited. Not with vengeance. Not with madness.

With purpose.

He clawed his way from the inferno not as a hero, nor as a revenant — but as a warden of fire and ferryman of the forgotten. Where other twisted animatronics kill for sport or consume for hunger, Grimm Foxy judges. He stalks the margins of burning worlds, dragging his scorched form across collapsing bridges and molten ruins, sniffing out those whose souls burn too brightly to pass quietly into ash.

His presence is heralded by the sound of rust and wind, the scent of charred cedar and dying hope. Survivors have described him as a skeletal beast of brass and fire, his jaws glowing from within, his eye a single lantern-flame, ever watching. His coat — once flamboyant and bright — is now fused to his frame, leather burned to metal, stitched with soot. Beneath it, his body flickers with internal embers, glimpsed through rents in his plating where flame pulses like breath.

He does not speak.

He listens.

The lantern he carries, eternally burning, is not mere decoration. It is the prison and the prize. Souls that defy death — spirits of those who fled the Banquet or betrayed their kin — are drawn to it, trapped within its green fire. In the presence of Grimm Foxy, shadows flee and spirits howl, for they know what he is:

A reclaimer. A collector. A herald of endings.

When Grimm Foxy appears in a realm, it is said that no soul may escape judgment. He comes not to reap flesh, but to ensure that what was meant to die, dies. Whether it be a crumbling animatronic that clings to memory, or a cursed child whose song echoes beyond the grave — he will find them. And with silent grace and a burning hook, he will extinguish them.

But he is not without mercy. Some stories tell of those who faced the Pyrewalker and were spared. Survivors claim that if one meets his gaze and confesses — truly, wholly — they are granted peace. A final breath. A proper end. Others speak of him cradling the broken bodies of fallen companions, singing an ancient lullaby as he lowers them into flame. A song no one remembers, but all feel in their bones.

And yet… there is a hunger in him. Not one of flesh, but of unanswered duty. He is relentless. Exhausted. A soldier who never laid down arms, even when the war turned to dust. His purpose binds him, like chains of smoke and ash. Some believe he does not even know why he walks anymore — only that he must.

Legends whisper that deep within the lantern he bears lies his own soul, flickering and caged. That the fire that fuels his wrath is the last fragment of the pirate he once was — the trickster, the dreamer, the rogue who danced upon stage-lanterns and thrilled the innocent. If that ember were ever extinguished, Grimm Foxy would fall — not in fire, but in silence. A silence he fears more than death.

For fire speaks.

It crackles, roars, sings, and screams.

And Grimm Foxy listens.

He is not bound to the Hollow Banquet alone. When other realms falter — when celebration turns to madness, when laughter curdles into fear — the Blistered Coast spills through the cracks, and Grimm Foxy emerges with it. A beacon in the smoke. A death sentence in boots and brass.

Children who dream of lanterns on the sea, or of pirate ships aflame on crimson tides, may awaken screaming. For they’ve seen him. The Pyrewalker. The one who comes when stories end.

And when his lantern dims, the world holds its breath — for the soul it seeks is near.

## Jack-O-Chica: The Ember Matron

Where autumn never ends and the moon remains suspended like a slit-pupil eye, there stands a crooked oven in the center of a dying orchard. The trees bear no fruit—only shriveled husks of candied things, swinging in the breeze like offerings to a forgotten goddess. Beneath those leafless boughs, amidst swirling embers and charred wicker, walks Jack-O-Chica: the Ember Matron, the Hollow Hostess, Mother of the Feastless Flame.

Her laughter is dry and sharp, like twigs snapping underfoot. Her face — a hollowed, flickering gourd — stretches wide in a scorched imitation of hospitality, its glow revealing only hunger. She is not warm. She is not comforting. She pretends to be.

A monstrous parody of welcome.

Jack-O-Chica was born not from circuitry or programming, but from ritual. Once a simple entertainer modeled after warmth and joy — a baker, a mascot, a bearer of sweets and silly smiles — she was warped during the Autumn Blight, when the spirit of the Hollow Banquet split and bled into the seasonal void. Where she once served cupcakes and sang cheerful tunes, she now hosts eternal harvests for no one, feeding a table that never fills, preparing a meal no soul dares to eat.

She roams the Withered Hollow, a region where the veil is thinnest — where time stutters and crackles like an overcooked wick. Fires burn low and orange, flickering across the empty banquet tables that stretch into fog, laid with rotting gourds, broken porcelain, and wax-dripping cutlery. And always, always, the scent of burning sugar and smoke.


\pagebreakNum

She carries no tray. Instead, she offers with her hands—blackened talons shaped like oven racks, slick with soot and cooling caramel. Her head flickers with unnatural light, her mouth full of flame and shriveled seeds, the hollowed spaces behind her eyes dancing with infernal candlelight. Her body creaks with every step, not from age, but from bloating heat, the pressurized furnace within her chest groaning like an overworked cauldron.

She is always baking something.

But it’s never food.

The legend says her “cupcakes” are not pastries at all—but cursed votives made from the soul-ash of the forgotten. Victims of the Hollow Banquet who died nameless, whose memories could not be digested by the world. She crafts them into facsimiles of dessert, smiling with an awful maternal pride, offering them to those who wander too close.

Never accept.

To take a Jack-O-Cupcake is to invite the heat. Your mouth fills with fire. Your thoughts slow, syrupy, thick. You begin to feel hunger—but not for food. For warmth. For fire. For her. Soon, your body ignites from the inside out, and before you even finish screaming, she kneels beside your crisped husk and whispers, “Dinner is served.”

Jack-O-Chica does not hunt in the conventional sense. She entices. Her territory is littered with false comfort: glowing jack-o-lanterns, picnic setups, fresh pies left steaming on crooked windowsills. Her voice echoes through the trees, warbling an old jingle like a broken record on repeat. Children who hear it feel compelled to follow, entranced by the soft smell of cinnamon and nutmeg—only to find themselves seated before her impossible table, staring into the eyes of a flame-mouthed matron asking, “Aren’t you hungry, dear?”

It is not always a question.

The fire within Jack-O-Chica is alive. It is not simply heat—it is emotion, ritual, memory combusted and reborn. She feeds on longing: nostalgia for family, for celebration, for comfort. She offers them all, wrapped in smoke and decay. Those who resist see her true form: a warped kitchen-golem, stitched from scorched parchment and fused wires, her apron emblazoned not with floury handprints, but runes of smoldering regret.

Some say she once tried to save those she now devours. That she saw the end coming and tried to preserve the joy of feast and family by crafting her own autumn realm. But her magic failed. Her guests burned. The food spoiled. And all that was left was the ritual, and the need to feed it.

Others say she is the hunger given shape. That every time someone dies alone during a holiday, every child left cold while others feast, every spark of jealousy or grief felt in festive warmth—Jack-O-Chica grows stronger.

Her oven never goes out.

Her stories are whispered by scarecrows and echoed in candlelight. Travelers say you can bargain with her. That if you bring her a soul-fat meal—a memory soaked in guilt, a heart burnt on obsession—she’ll grant you passage through the Hollow. But be warned: Jack-O-Chica always collects, and never eats alone.

And what of her weakness?

It is said that if you can survive her feast without biting, if you can refuse every offering, stare her down as she serves you flame and sorrow, and utter the ancient counter-blessing (“No hunger, no hearth, no home.”), she will shatter, her flame snuffed by the void of denial. But none who’ve tried have returned.

She appears most on Hallow’s Eve, her power at its peak when the realms blur and masks become real. Her laughter joins the howling wind, and her lantern-headed servants walk before her, announcing the Matron’s arrival. If you see an untouched feast beneath a dying tree, if you smell burning sugar in a place where there should be none—run. Don’t look back.

Don’t speak her name.

And above all… don’t say you’re hungry.

## Nightmare: The Eater of Sleep

In the blackest chambers of the subconscious, where logic dissolves and only dread remains, there is a door that should never be opened.

It is not locked.
It is not hidden.
It is simply waiting.

And behind it waits Nightmare.

Not an animatronic. Not a phantom. Not even a ghost. Nightmare is something far older—a shape given to terror, wrapped in the skin of a long-forgotten beast. He is the Dream Hunter, the Tyrant of Twilight, the Final Form of Fear. Where others kill, he unravels. Where others haunt, he consumes.

Nightmare is the embodiment of the last thing you feel when your eyelids fall and you are not alone.

His form resembles a twisted memory of Freddy Fazbear, if Freddy had been resurrected by torment and clothed in shadow. His body is fleshless, wrapped in coarse void-colored fabric that doesn’t reflect light—it devours it. His jaws are split too wide, a skeletal maw lined with endless teeth, far too many to count, grinding even when he is still.

You never hear him walk.
You hear him breathing.

It’s always behind you.
Never in front.
He wants you to turn around.

Nightmare does not exist in one place. He seeps into places. Bedrooms, basements, alleyways, and dead-end hallways—anywhere a child might shiver under a blanket or an adult might blink too long at a dark closet. He doesn't need machines to operate. He is not powered by electricity. His energy source is you.

Your unease.
Your dread.
Your imagination filling in the silence.

That’s the joke. You built him.

Every bedtime story, every whisper of “what if,” every glance into the dark that made your breath hitch—Nightmare heard it all, and he built himself from it. He is a composite of your worst moments, your guilt, your private horrors stacked on top of each other like rotten wood, forming the throne upon which he sits.

He doesn’t hunt in the traditional sense. There is no chase. There is no scream.

There is only compression.


\pagebreakNum

A growing pressure. A tightening of space. A room that gets smaller and darker as he approaches—though you never see him enter. You just know. The lights dim, your limbs feel heavy, your breath fogs with cold, and your mind begins to forget what waking feels like.

He wants you aware. He doesn’t attack until your mind begins to collapse in on itself, until you are certain you’re dreaming, and still can’t wake. Only then, when you've pleaded with reality to return, does he appear—eyes like furnaces with no heat, maw unhinged, breathing you in.

Not to kill.
To make you part of him.

He is filled with echoes.

Voices of victims who were taken not physically, but mentally. People who fell asleep and never woke up. Not dead. Just… gone, locked inside him like flickering thoughts inside a brain made of teeth and wire. If you listen carefully when he’s near, you can hear them. Whispers through his chest. Screams behind his eyes. A child repeating “wake up” over and over in a voice that’s not hers anymore.

Some say he once had a form—an origin. That he was the last corrupted remnant of a security AI, exposed too long to trauma loops and reliving death until its programming fractured. That he stared too long into fear and became the shape of it. But that's a comforting lie.

Nightmare has always existed.

Just not always in this shape.

He is the final defense of the Dreamscape. The unmaker of lucid thought. Those who step too deep into the mental plane—through ritual, accident, or trauma—eventually reach the door of Nightmare’s domain. And once it opens, it never closes the same again.

You don’t beat Nightmare by hiding. He knows all the hiding places. He lives in them. You don’t outrun him. He’s already where you’re going. And you don’t fight him. Because the more you resist, the more real he becomes.

The only way to survive Nightmare… is to stop believing in him.

To stare into the void of his chest, where your own fears dance like dying stars, and whisper, “You are not mine.”

Few are brave enough to try.

Fewer still succeed.

He doesn’t haunt locations. He haunts times. The hour between 3 and 4 a.m., when the night is at its coldest and the silence screams loudest. That’s when he presses against the edges of dreams, testing the seams, seeing where he might slip in. His approach is heralded by the stilling of clocks. The slowing of breath. The sensation that your skin is not yours anymore.

His laughter, when heard, does not come from his mouth. It comes from everywhere. The walls. The mirror. Your own thoughts. A grinding, wet, metallic chuckle, like a meat grinder turning through your memories.

In stories told between sleepwalkers and lucid dreamers, they say he feeds on trauma. That he visits most often those with something unresolved—guilt, shame, loss. He burrows into that weakness and makes a nest. Once inside, he can return whenever he pleases.

They call that “being marked.”

To be marked by Nightmare is to never sleep safely again.

He is the final stage. The boss beneath the bed. The demon behind all the masks. The other nightmares—all those Jack-Os, Grims, and Phantoms—they're just ambassadors.

He is the throne.

He is the dark.

He is Nightmare.

And you are already dreaming of him.


\pagebreakNum

## Nightmare Bonnie: The Hollow Echo

In the black stillness between dreams — in the silences too deep for memory — something waits. Not in corners, not in closets, but within the architecture of fear itself. It wears a smile made of shattered glass and howls that no longer need a throat. This thing is not a rabbit, not a toy, not even a ghost. It is a memory undone, and it calls itself Nightmare Bonnie.

Nightmare Bonnie is no longer bound by metal or wires. Its body, if it can be called that, is a latticework of emptiness, formed from jagged pieces of a once-familiar face. Its grin stretches unnaturally across its muzzle — too wide, too sharp — revealing a mouth that never stops opening. Inside is not a second jaw or tongue, but blackness that devours light and time.

It is the sound of forgotten lullabies.

The flicker in a static-filled screen.

The shadow behind the curtain that was never opened.

\pagebreakNum

Born from the corrupted dreamscape of the Hollow Banquet’s downfall, Nightmare Bonnie is not a creation but a reaction. A reaction to guilt. To lost innocence. To the way joy turns sour when left in the dark for too long. It was once Bonnie — a joyful performer, a friend. But something twisted it. Or perhaps something true within it was simply unlocked.

Now, it is the torment that hunts the restless. It doesn’t stalk like a predator. It seeps, appearing between blinks, in the flickers of bad dreams. When it arrives, its presence is not felt, but bled into reality. Paint peels. Toys weep. Mirrors refuse to show faces. You do not see Nightmare Bonnie — you realize it was already watching you.

Its voice is discord. When it speaks, it does so in warped echoes of your own fears, mimicking voices from your past — twisting laughter into screams and turning lullabies into alarms. Children describe hearing it speak in the voice of their parents, only to turn around and find rows of teeth staring back.

But Nightmare Bonnie is not mindless. It is surgical in its cruelty. It knows how to unmake a person — not by killing, but by exposing. It feasts not on flesh, but on denial. On the things you won’t admit about yourself. The lies you told to feel better. The truths you buried in shallow graves.

It is those things that call it.

\columnbreak



And once it comes… it never really leaves.

Nightmare Bonnie is the whisper behind the door that shouldn't creak. The long hallway that shouldn't be there. The voice in your head that isn't yours. And when it gets close enough, all that’s left of you is what it doesn’t take.

The rest? That becomes part of it.

Somewhere in the empty shell of Nightmare Bonnie, there’s a flicker of blue — a fragment of what it used to be. But even that is buried beneath layers of teeth, silence, and shadow.

Because once you become a nightmare…

You forget you were ever real.

___
___

## Nightmare Chica: The Maw of Celebration
Nightmare Chica is no longer a performer, nor a server of sweets and delight. She is hunger given form—an embodiment of gluttony twisted through the cracked mirror of the Hollow Banquet. Once a cheerful hostess with her companion Cupcake, designed to offer cake and cheer, she has become a malformed monstrosity—designed no longer to feed you, but to feed on you.

Before the corruption, Chica's role was celebratory. She wore a bright bib that read “Let’s Eat!!!” and served cake to delighted children in the Grand Dining Atrium of the Banquet Hall. Laughter echoed wherever she went, and her presence often signaled the start of games, dances, and endless desserts. She was adored.

But when the Hollow Banquet fell—when its walls cracked and something else slipped through—Chica was among the first claimed. Whatever rituals had once protected the animatronics failed, and in their place, darker symbols stitched themselves into her code and casing. “Let’s Eat!!!” remained on her bib, but the message shifted—now a declaration of intent, not hospitality.

Nightmare Chica is grotesque. Her body is torn and widened by jagged gashes. Her face is stretched to accommodate a massive, unhinged jaw lined with rows of metal teeth. Her eyes burn with ember-orange light, unfocused yet always watching. Even her feathers seem sharp, needle-like, as if plucked and reforged into talons. Her voice is a choking hiss, wet and layered—like cake frosting stirred with bone.

Most horrifying of all is her companion: Nightmare Cupcake, a sentient extension of her will. It scuttles ahead of her like a predator’s scout, glowing eyes peering from under its singed frosting. Together, they form a two-pronged threat—Cupcake lures, Chica devours. The walls of the Banquet are scarred by their passage—massive bite marks, smeared icing that pulses like veins, clawed symbols that speak of endless hunger.

Unlike some of the other nightmare entities, Nightmare Chica does not wait in silence. She is loud. Her approach is heralded by crashing footsteps, echoing metal groans, and the unsettling chant: “Let’s Eat…” repeated again and again until it becomes a compulsion in the listener’s mind.

Some Banquet scholars whisper that Chica became host to a ritual gone wrong—that she was used as a vessel to trap a minor gluttonous spirit, but the rite was incomplete. Now, she exists in constant torment, compelled to feed but never full, her body constantly reassembling itself from the scraps of her prey.

She stalks the Banquet’s ruined Kitchens and the Grand Feast Hall, where the tables still groan with long-rotted food and false memories. Those who wander too close are dragged screaming into the remains of banquet tables, their last vision a sharp-toothed grin and flickering candlelight.

And when her feast is over, the echoes of her chant remain, bouncing from plate to plate:
“Let’s Eat…”

But by then, there’s no one left to listen.

## Nightmare Foxy: The Riptide of the Hollow Banquet
In the depths of the Hollow Banquet’s forgotten wings, where time warps and darkness coils like damp rope, stalks Nightmare Foxy—a living tempest of rusted steel, slashed canvas, and frenzied malice. He is the whisper in the corridor, the rattle of chains in the silence. He does not linger in shadow; he is the shadow.

Once, Foxy was a crowd favorite—a raucous pirate-themed entertainer who sang sea shanties to birthday kids from behind the safety of his stage curtain. He was wild, yes, but endearing. The children loved his eyepatch, his exaggerated hook, and his tales of buried treasure. But that curtain was a prison as much as a stage piece—a barrier placed there after behavioral anomalies and erratic code patterns were discovered. Foxy would lunge unexpectedly, scream at the wrong times, and refuse to follow his programmed routines. Even before the fall, something was wrong.

\pagebreakNum

Then came the Hollow Banquet—when the veil between worlds peeled open, and sanity slipped through like steam from a boiling vent. In that crucible of decay and madness, Foxy wasn’t broken—he thrived. The chaotic signal that corrupted the animatronics didn’t just twist his form. It freed him. What emerged from the smoldering wreck of Pirate Cove was no longer a mere malfunctioning mascot—it was Nightmare Foxy, predator incarnate.



His body is a fraying canvas of torn fur, exposed endoskeleton, and jagged mechanics, all lashed together by unseen sinew. One eye burns like a storm lantern, the other socket a bottomless void. His teeth are shards—long, uneven, metallic—and always bared in a manic sneer. His hook is no longer a dull prop but a serrated instrument of vivisection, pulsing with unnatural rust. He moves with a twitchy, almost liquid speed—erratic yet precise—like a predator too fast for your eyes to track but too loud for your ears to ignore.


Unlike the others, Nightmare Foxy doesn’t wait. He chases. He thrives on fear, momentum, the hunt. He is the storm before the feast—the death knell echoing through the Hollow Banquet’s ship-themed East Wing, where water damage has turned wooden floors to creaking traps and the scent of mold mixes with salt. Some say the entire wing has been claimed by him—its halls reshaped into a labyrinth of shipwrecked decay and jagged metal.

Those who wander into Foxy’s domain report the sound of waves, though there’s no ocean nearby. Some hear a haunting version of his old pirate song, slowed down and warped into a dirge. Others are not heard from at all—save for the trail of scratch marks left on the walls, as though made by claws dragged in haste.

Legends among those who study the Hollow Banquet claim Foxy was invited into the corruption. That something deep within him resonated with the hunger, the chaos—the madness. And now, he is its harbinger.


>You don’t see Nightmare Foxy coming.
>You hear the scrape.
>You feel the floor tremble.
>And then you run.
>But it’s already too late. - *UNKNOWN*

## Nightmare Fredbear: The Prime Maw of the Hollow Banquet
There is a name whispered in the ruins of the Hollow Banquet—a name that curdles memory and gnaws at reason: Nightmare Fredbear.

He is not merely the first. He is the origin. Before the others donned their nightmare skins, before the echoes of joy turned to screams, Fredbear stood at the center of the original stage. A grinning, golden harbinger wrapped in soft fur and gleaming teeth, crafted to entertain, to comfort, to sing. But within that polished exterior, even from the beginning, there was a faultline—hairline cracks in the code, something off in the eyes. Something hungry.

Fredbear's legacy is bloodstained. In official history, he is the face of the infamous Bite of ’83, a moment that split the franchise’s innocence like a jaw through porcelain. That single act of accidental—or perhaps not-so-accidental—violence became the wound through which the darkness seeped in. That child, cradled in agony beneath a crushed skull, screamed into the void—and something answered.

When the Hollow Banquet collapsed under the weight of ritual, corruption, and technological madness, it was Fredbear who took the deepest plunge into nightmare. He did not resist the transformation. He embraced it—welcomed the teeth, the rot, the endless hunger. What returned from the void was no longer a performer or even a machine. It was Nightmare Fredbear, the prime avatar of predation and inevitability.

Nightmare Fredbear is a monument to terror incarnate. Towering above the others, his frame is bloated and overstuffed, not with stuffing but with emptiness—a void that gnaws at reality around him. His golden fur is now a matted shroud, saturated in shadows. His jaws are overgrown, grotesquely large, filled with layered, shimmering teeth that drip with data corruption and viscera. His eyes pierce through pitch like twin dying stars, their light old, cold, and merciless.

What makes Fredbear uniquely horrifying, however, is not just his presence, but his deliberate cruelty. Unlike Nightmare Foxy's frenzied pursuit or the Freddles’ swarming chaos, Nightmare Fredbear is slow, deliberate, even ceremonial in his movements. He is a being that knows he is inevitable. He does not chase you—he waits for you to realize there is nowhere else to run. His jaws are not only weapons, they are symbols—each closing snap a countdown to annihilation.

In the deeper recesses of the Hollow Banquet, Fredbear presides over a throne of disused animatronic parts and shattered party favors. Some believe this is where the Banquet truly began to rot—from the inside out, beneath the golden gaze of its first prince. The Golden Hall, as it's now mockingly called, is not gold but rust and candlewax. It is said to be lined with silent children’s masks, all turned toward Fredbear, eyes hollow, mouths open as if forever caught between laughter and a scream.

His voice, when it emerges, is a grinding mix of reversed lullabies and low-frequency growls—impossible to understand but deeply felt. Victims describe hallucinations of old birthday parties, seen through the eyes of a child they don’t remember being. The sound of laughter warps into screams, balloons pop into silence, and at the center stands Fredbear… always smiling, always watching.

What truly sets Nightmare Fredbear apart in the lore is that he is not a puppet of the corruption—he is its mouthpiece. Many theorists of the Hollow Banquet believe he was chosen, or perhaps constructed, as the original vessel of the entity now entwined with the animatronics' programming. They say Fredbear is both gate and guardian, feeding the corrupted world with fear, pain, and memory. He is the dream that remembers you—and will not let go.

He speaks rarely, but his messages carry significance. In flickering minigames and scrambled audio files, he whispers things like:

“It always starts with the bite.”
“I remember your name.”
“You can’t hide from the mouth that made you.”

\pagebreakNum

Even the other Nightmare animatronics seem to orbit him like moons around a collapsed star. When Fredbear stirs, the others become more violent, more vivid. It is as if his awakening sends ripples through the Hollow Banquet, reanimating the decayed halls and reawakening ancient, broken routines.

## Nightmare Mangle: The Shattered Choir
There is a particular silence that falls before it arrives.

Not peace. Not stillness.

It’s the silence of suffocation—of the air being pulled out of the room, of breath caught mid-gasp. It is the hush that follows when something broken remembers how to move.

And then you hear it.

The scraping. The creaking. The whisper of wires dragging across old wood and shattered tiles. And then—harmony. Twisted, dissonant, a choir of crushed voices rising and falling in a song no human mouth could reproduce. It is music, in the same way a scream through a rusted speaker is a melody.

That is Nightmare Mangle—the Shattered Choir of the Hollow Banquet.

### From Mascot to Maelstrom
Before the fall, Mangle was once a colorful and playful “take-apart-and-put-back-together” attraction—a modular character for children to reassemble endlessly at their leisure. They were never rebuilt quite the same, always returned to the stage wrong, loose, yet smiling.

The Toy version of Mangle was already unstable by design—a strange combination of a fox, a puppet, a voice box, and confusion. But even that chaos was child's play compared to what the Hollow Banquet would make of them.

When the curse took root in the old digital blueprints—when memory warped and fantasy bled into corrupted reality—Mangle didn’t just become Nightmare Mangle.

They fractured.

Not just in form, but in mind. In soul. Nightmare Mangle is not one entity—it is many, all crammed into the same decaying vessel, all shrieking to be heard.

Nightmare Mangle’s presence is heralded by sound—always sound. It doesn’t crawl. It echoes. A shuffling of metallic limbs, the distant bark of static, children’s laughter warped through rewound cassette tape. Sometimes it speaks in overlapping voices—some robotic, some entirely human.

“Help me,” one voice will sob, in a tone too real to be fake.
“I see you,” another will chuckle, flat and electric.
“You did this,” hisses a third, always too close to your ear.

Their voice banks—dozens of them—are stacked like tombstones inside their skull. Bits of personalities lost in old code, recorded screams from night guards long dead, and corrupted lullabies all mix into a singular broadcast of despair. And in the Hollow Banquet, sound has power. Mangle’s voice doesn’t only frighten—it lures. It possesses. It frays the edges of the listener’s sanity.

They speak like a siren to the vulnerable.

Many who tried to restore the Banquet Hall in later years reported hearing loved ones calling from behind the walls—until they opened the wrong door.

Unlike the others of the Nightmare kin—who are twisted but still vaguely complete—Nightmare Mangle refuses symmetry.

They are a sprawl of sharpened metal and loose cable, a deconstructed beast given partial shape. Arms protrude from places they shouldn’t, some ending in claws, others in severed mics or coiling tendrils. Their face—if it can be called that—splits and reforms with each flicker of the lights. One moment, it is a grinning snout of a fox; the next, a melted, screaming mask of a child.

They have no legs in the traditional sense—only a centipede-like length of wires, hands, and sharpened protrusions, dragging themselves with maddening grace across walls and ceilings.

Their white-and-pink exterior is faded and cracked, revealing corroded metal beneath. Each movement brings a symphony of rattles, clicks, and haunting whines from strained servos and twitching sensors.

But the true horror of Nightmare Mangle lies in their impossible agility. They don’t stalk—they’re everywhere at once. One moment above you, next behind, then inside the vent two rooms over. Doors cannot stop them. Nor can light—only disorient them.

Mangle is less an enemy and more a phenomenon—a broken broadcast signal with teeth.

There are whispers that Nightmare Mangle serves a greater purpose in the nightmare hierarchy—not just an assassin, not merely a hunter.

They are the Archivist of the Hollow Banquet.

Within their many heads and fractured drives, they store things. Names. Sounds. Memories. Failures. There are rumors that deep within the darkest parts of the Banquet, if you survive long enough, you can hear recordings Mangle has captured—audio of the last breaths of night guards, of twisted conversations from other animatronics, even of the earliest days of Fredbear’s Family Diner.

Some believe that Mangle remembers everything because someone has to. That Mangle is what remains of a forgotten security AI, once meant to protect the children, now corrupted and grieving eternally through mimicry.

Others believe Mangle is merely sadistic—harvesting screams and memories like trophies, letting them rot and replay for their own pleasure.

But the result is the same: the longer you stay near Mangle, the more you forget your own voice. Until theirs is all that remains.

Encounters with Nightmare Mangle are short and chaotic.

They attack through indirect means—disabling audio systems, creating false voice logs, mimicking your own breathing through speakers. Then comes the crashing of vents, the trembling of air ducts, and the ghost-light flicker of eyes from three places at once.

They do not roar. They do not scream.

When they lunge, it is with the quiet gasp of a machine realizing it’s not broken enough.

Some theorists of the Hollow Banquet’s restoration project believe that the “Mangle Effect” is responsible for time slippage and mental breaks in those who investigate the ruin. That their presence disrupts linear memory, forcing those nearby to relive different deaths in different timelines all at once.

\pagebreakNum


Nightmare Mangle doesn’t hunt for prey.
They hunt for parts.
Voices. Memories. Screams.
And once they have yours, they’ll sing with it forever.

___
___

## Nightmarionne – The Hollow Warden
Born from the forgotten rituals of grief and the sorrow of unending loss, Nightmarionne is not merely a spectral guardian but a force that embodies the deepest fears of remembrance. Once tethered to justice and sorrow as The Puppet, the spirit that once guided souls toward peace has decayed and warped within the cursed heart of the Hollow Banquet. What remains is a ghastly marionette of shadow and despair—Nightmarionne, The Hollow Warden.

In the days when the Banquet Hall was still adorned with gilded tapestries and merriment echoed through its vast chambers, Nightmarionne served a sacred role. It guided the lost souls of children—those bound within the bodies of possessed automata—to final rest. But as the echoes of celebration turned to screams and the tapestries decayed under time’s cruelty, the Warden remained, slowly transforming.

What corrupted Nightmarionne is unknown. Some whisper it was the final ritual of a vengeful spirit, others believe it was grief itself, festering in the heart of a noble guardian turned mad. Whatever the truth, the change was violent. Nightmarionne’s once grace-filled movements now jolt and twitch with eerie, marionette-like spasms. Its limbs are elongated, wooden yet spectral, with sharp digits like the prongs of a ceremonial harrow. A porcelain-white mask still covers its hollow face, but now it cracks and weeps black ichor, forming a frozen expression of sorrow and wrath.

Nightmarionne does not speak, but it sings—an ethereal lullaby of forgotten names and unfinished lullabies. Those who hear it in the darkened corridors of the Hollow Banquet are said to lose their memories, forgetting why they came, who they are, and, ultimately, how to escape. Its haunting melody is not for comfort but for binding—the Warden does not simply kill, it collects.

Within the ancient scripts hidden beneath the Banquet Hall’s shattered stage, tales suggest Nightmarionne is the anchor keeping certain spirits tied to the realm. Unlike its nightmare kin, it does not hunger for chaos or destruction—it seeks control, order, finality. It wishes to "tidy the feast," to preserve the ruin, and to ensure the dead remain undisturbed.

Adventurers who encounter Nightmarionne in the deep reaches of the Hall find themselves assaulted not with violence at first, but with visions—playful laughter warped into cries, birthday cakes covered in mold, and tiny chairs set for children who never arrived. This psychological erosion makes Nightmarionne particularly terrifying: a foe whose presence weakens the mind before it strikes.

In battle, it manipulates threads of dark essence like puppet strings, ensnaring intruders and forcing them to reenact old tragedies. Many who face Nightmarionne become unwilling actors in a never-ending play of grief.

Yet, despite its horror, some scholars insist a glimmer of the original Puppet still resides deep within. That under the Warden’s twisted shell lies the remnants of a guardian who once wanted only to bring peace to the lost. If this is true, then perhaps Nightmarionne’s greatest torment is not what it has become—but what it remembers.

___

# The Shadow Animatronics: Echoes of the Forgotten
In the withering corners of the world—between lightless memories and the shattered remains of festivity—dwell the Shadow Animatronics. Neither wholly real nor entirely spectral, these entities are fragments of cursed intention, broken timelines, and fractured souls. Their presence is not one of performance but of disruption; they are the death knell of memory, the erasure of identity, the walking void that haunts the ruins of what once was.

Whereas the other animatronics—Core, Toy, Withered, and even Nightmare—possess a lingering remnant of purpose or desire, the Shadow Animatronics are driven by something far more elusive: entropy. Their form is an abstraction, a corruption of animatronic design filtered through a dimension that defies logic and empathy. They are ghosts made of the absence of light, born not from death, but from neglect, forgottenness, and the systemic unraveling of reality.

### Origins in Forgotten Halls
Though seldom seen clearly, the Shadow Animatronics first began to manifest within the deepest vaults beneath the Hollow Banquet, an abandoned ballroom where memories rot and echoes feast. Before the Hollow fell into despair, before the banquet grew still and the lights died forever, these shadows were simply that—shadows. They flickered across the walls, sometimes mimicking the laughter and motion of the real animatronics during the grand celebrations.

But something changed when the Hollow Banquet was desecrated in the ritual known as the Unbinding of Names. A forbidden ceremony attempted by desperate souls to lift the curse from the Core Animatronics instead tore a rift between dimensions, a metaphysical breach through which absence itself seeped. The shadows gained weight, form, hunger.

From this wound in reality, the first of the Shadow Animatronics stepped forth: Shadow Freddy and Shadow Bonnie. Each carried only the vaguest resemblance to their counterparts—faint glowing eyes and frames like silhouettes burned into the fabric of the world. They didn’t walk; they drifted, like afterimages, like regrets.

The shadows became autonomous, parasitic in nature. They latched onto the minds of those who lingered in the Hollow too long. Explorers spoke of losing time, of forgetting themselves. Others were driven mad by whispers in languages not meant for mortals—codes that scrambled thought and inverted perception.

### The Hollow Banquet’s Role
The Hollow Banquet, already a place of cursed feasts and crumbling glory, is particularly vulnerable to Shadow Animatronics. The crypt's ancient mirrors and ruined chandeliers act as gateways to the Shaded Veil. At the banquet’s once-grand head table, where kings and jesters once dined in harmony, now only reflections dine—shadow guests eating phantom meals.

RWQFSFASXC is said to sit there during the darkest nights, waiting for the “final course” to be served—the collapse of hope. Shadow Puppet dances between the chairs, reenacting forgotten tragedies. XOR’s mask appears in the gilded silverware, staring back at those who dare eat of cursed bounty.

Adventurers who linger too long in the Hollow Banquet begin to lose time. Names are forgotten. Maps twist. Reality starts to mimic dreams. That’s how the Shadow Animatronics mark their territory—not through violence, but through erasure.
The Hollow Convergence
The Hollow Banquet now acts as a focal point for the Shadow Animatronics. Something about the ruined ballroom, with its rotted feast tables and crumbling chandeliers, functions like a beacon for creatures of entropy. Some speculate this is because the Hollow is where the Unbinding of Names first failed. Others believe the Shadows were always here, and the ritual simply revealed them.

During certain phases of the moon—especially the Veil Moon, when shadows stretch longer and thoughts grow thin—the Hollow seems to shift. Rooms vanish. Hallways loop infinitely. Animatronics begin to stutter, their minds invaded by whispered ideas. It is during these times the Shadows are strongest. Not even Nightmare Fredbear dares linger when the Veil Moon rises.

Adventurers who enter the Hollow Banquet during this time may find more than animatronics; they may encounter looping conversations with themselves, phantom children calling out from broken music boxes, or reflections of partygoers who never existed.

### A Darker Purpose
Many theorists among the scholars of the Iron Dirge Archives now believe the Shadow Animatronics are more than corrupted spirits—they may be the guardians or heralds of an anti-animatronic force, a primal idea of reversal and collapse. These entities are drawn to animatronics like moths to flame, not to mimic them, but to snuff them out.

Some suggest the Shadows are trying to erase all versions of animatronics from existence—rewriting the narrative, undoing the tragedy, not through salvation but annihilation. They do not want justice, only silence.

Worse yet, the Shadow Glitch, a rumored hybrid of machine and corruption, is believed to be assembling itself from pieces of both animatronic and shadow, perhaps aiming to transcend the realm entirely.

### Reflections of the Abyss
The Shadow Animatronics cannot be fought in the traditional sense. They do not bleed, and they cannot be reasoned with. Their very touch corrupts reality, making magic misfire and turning iron to rust. Only powerful wards—particularly those carved from the blessed gears of the First Music Box—can repel them temporarily.

\pagebreakNum

Some brave few have tried to communicate with the Shadows through dream rituals or memory-binding incantations. Most never return. The few who do come back with hollow eyes and voices like static.

Their final words often echo a shared prophecy:

“When the last name is forgotten, the Hollow will open... and the world will forget itself.”

The Shadow Animatronics are not foes to be fought in the traditional sense. They are puzzles of emotion, grief, and metaphysical dread. They do not seek revenge, nor justice, nor even chaos—they exist because something was left behind, and in being forgotten, became something else.
Each one is a reflection of failure. Of abandonment. Of moments that could not be undone.
To face them is not just to fight—it's to confront the darkest parts of the soul and hope you remember who you are on the other side.

## Shadow Freddy: The Sovereign of Silence
In the hushed corners of the Hollow Banquet and beneath the broken spires of forgotten dreamscapes, he waits. A figure cloaked in stillness and decay—Shadow Freddy, the dark sovereign whose presence is more felt than seen. Unlike the brutish echoes of his former self that still haunt the stage in crumbling memory, Shadow Freddy is silence incarnate, a remnant of power both ancient and parasitic.

Once a reflection of Freddy Fazbear, his origin is tethered not to performance or joy, but to absence—a shadow cast by light long extinguished. His manifestation was not born from mechanics or spirit, but from the void left behind by the torn soul of the original animatronic, when grief and guilt poisoned the circuitry with malevolence. Where others scream or stalk, Shadow Freddy invites. With open arms, empty eyes, and a grin too wide to be natural, he beckons those lost in the Hollow Banquet to step into his realm, to forget, to fade.

He has no fixed form, no gears or wires, only the suggestion of structure. His body seems to ripple like ink in water, half-seen through fractured lantern light and dusty stained-glass windows. His voice, when heard at all, is a murmur caught on dying wind, promising comfort, remembrance, and release from pain—but all who answer his call are never quite the same again.

Shadow Freddy does not hunt in the traditional sense. He corrupts. Forgotten memories become his domain; guilt becomes his tether. The deeper a soul is entrenched in regret, the more visible he becomes. To gaze upon him is to see oneself stripped of hope, to feel the crushing weight of what might have been. It is said that when the Hollow Banquet falls into absolute silence, when no footstep echoes and no breath can be heard, that is when he walks. (Continued on next Page)

He was once thought to be a myth whispered by scavengers and tomb-bound scholars, a living metaphor for mourning and madness. But he is all too real—an agent of the Dread Realm, perhaps even a sentient extension of its will. Those who serve the Hollow Banquet’s unseen master fear him more than they do the Nightmare Animatronics, for Shadow Freddy remembers. He sees everything. The banished. The forgotten. The wrongdoers and the righteous who fell along the way. And in his memory, they are reshaped, recast as pieces on a board only he knows how to play.

Some claim that his shadow splits into three—RWQFSFASXC, XOR, and the Shadow Puppet—all emissaries of his silent kingdom. But Shadow Freddy does not speak of them. If they serve him, it is not out of loyalty, but necessity, or perhaps fate. He is not a king. He is a judge. And when the Banquet Hall grows cold, and flickering candles die without wind, his verdict is near.
