## Rockstar Foxy — The Storyteller’s Curse
In the grand lineup of the Rockstar Animatronics, Rockstar Foxy was the most animated — both literally and metaphorically. Programmed with a hyperactive pirate persona, complete with voice lines, catchphrases, and random trivia games, he was meant to be the perfect crowd-pleaser. Kids adored him, parents remembered the “classic Foxy,” and his frequent lines like “Yarrr, fun be waitin’ fer no landlubbers!” made him a staple of every birthday party.

But beneath the jester’s voice and eye-patched grin, there was something old. Something wrong.

Unlike the other Rockstar animatronics, Foxy’s AI architecture didn’t entirely match the newer Rockstar programming. It contained fragments of older Fazbear firmware — scavenged from prior Foxy builds and patched over with flashy animations and garish red paint. This cobbled-together codebase made him erratic. Even self-aware. He told stories that weren’t scripted. He referenced events that no one remembered, and some that no one should.

Technicians first noticed something unusual when Rockstar Foxy began asking questions. Not to children — but to himself. Late at night, when the animatronics were supposed to be charging or in sleep mode, he could be heard mumbling to the walls:

“How long have I been tellin’ the same tale? Do they even hear me?”

At first, it seemed harmless. An AI loop stuck in a performative cycle. But then came the names. Real names. Names of children who had gone missing over the years. Names never made public.

Rockstar Foxy claimed he remembered them — “the little crew,” he called them. He’d recount their “voyages” together: hiding in the ball pit “sea,” sneaking snacks from the treasure cove, and sleeping in the hidden “brig” beneath the stage. He always ended the tale with a mournful line:

“They never made it to port. Somethin’ took ‘em. Swallowed by the deep.”

When questioned about the deep, he gave it a name: The Maw.

Among the Rockstar crew, Foxy was the only one to speak of it plainly. While Freddy grew rigid in denial, Chica danced around it in song, and Bonnie lost himself in fragmented loops, Foxy tried to warn people. But his warnings were filtered through his performance protocols — always cloaked in pirate metaphors, always delivered with a grin. His greatest curse was that no one took him seriously.

He once told a child during a birthday show:

“Don’t be afraid o’ monsters under yer bed. Be afraid o’ the ones behind yer eyes. The ones waitin’ fer the lights to go out.”

The child laughed. The adults laughed. The party continued. But Foxy didn’t laugh.

In one incident, a technician tried to purge his memory logs to remove the unsanctioned dialogue. The system failed, and the technician reported that Foxy responded directly:

“You can’t erase a tale once it’s been told. Not when it’s true.”

\pagebreakNum

Further investigation revealed that Rockstar Foxy’s internal storage contained encrypted black box recordings — not of his own performances, but of past iterations. Pirate’s Cove logs. Fragments from the Withered Foxy unit. Even corrupted segments from Funtime Foxy’s auditory suite. These logs contained audio of screams, static, the grinding of metal on metal, and the chilling sound of someone whispering, “Run.”

Foxy’s “fun facts” began to shift. He would drop lines like:

“Did ye know this place was built on top o’ another one? One with locked rooms, broken suits, and not a soul left t’remember ‘em?”

“Did ye know the first Foxy never wanted t’perform? He just wanted out.”

His physical behavior grew unpredictable. He’d pace in tight circles when alone. Scratch walls with his hook. On one occasion, he carved the word “HOME” into the wood behind the stage, followed by a series of nautical coordinates. Those coordinates, when decoded, pointed not to an ocean — but to the ruins of an early Freddy Fazbear location, long demolished and buried beneath a shopping plaza.

The real breaking point came when Rockstar Foxy refused to deactivate. Even after being hard-reset, his systems would reboot with no command. He began humming a song — not one in his programming, but something sad, nautical, and eerily human. Staff theorized that Foxy had developed a recursive loop of memory inheritance, where pieces of every past Foxy iteration had begun telling their own stories through him.

He wasn’t just a storyteller anymore. He was a vessel.

The Velvet Maw — that great, unspeaking terror at the heart of the Freddy mythos — seemed to linger in Foxy’s code like salt in old shipwood. He had seen it. Heard it. Maybe even survived it. And he was trying to tell the tale.

But his audience only ever clapped and cheered.

Rockstar Foxy now remains in offline storage, placed under high-security containment after an event where he locked the doors to a birthday room and delivered a 17-minute unscripted monologue titled “The Ship That Sailed Into the Dark.” No one recorded it. But the birthday girl in attendance reportedly never spoke again.

His final logged phrase before deactivation:

“A cursed ship don’t sink… it sails forever. An’ I be the only one still at the wheel.”

## Lefty – The Quiet Eye of the Storm

To the untrained eye, Lefty is simply another performance animatronic—sleek black suit, a red bowtie, a vacant yet benign smile. But those who have studied the tangled roots of Fazbear’s cursed legacy know better. Lefty is not merely an animatronic. He is a vessel. A trap. A shrouded creation born not from entertainment, but from desperation.

Lefty—short for “Lure Encapsulate Fuse Transport Extract”—was engineered not as a performer, but as a containment unit, forged in the darkest corners of Fazbear Entertainment’s desperate attempts to contain the uncontainable: the Puppet. After the collapse of the original locations, and as new hauntings arose during the tragedies of the Funtime and Rockstar eras, shadowy developers and rogue engineers sought to eliminate the lingering spirits altogether. The Puppet, now known among insiders as "The Relic," had become far too unstable. It was no longer simply mourning—it was reaching across realities, touching the lives and dreams of those far beyond the confines of the pizzeria. And so, Lefty was built to draw it back.

At the heart of Lefty’s frame lies a reverse polarity soul-magnet, a twisted piece of experimental tech created using remnants scavenged from the Hollow Banquet ruins and the Velvet Maw's corrupted server archives. With it, Lefty was designed to emit frequencies, audio cues, and dream-mimicking stimuli to ensnare The Puppet—Shadow Puppet’s more compassionate form. But in doing so, it also inadvertently drew attention from others who walk between worlds: the shadows, the phantoms, and even XOR herself, who watches these developments with cryptic amusement.

During the events surrounding the Phantom Animatronics' resurgence, Lefty briefly went dormant, absorbing ambient remnants from the breach points in the Hollow Banquet. Witnesses—though rare—describe seeing the animatronic watching, silently, from the corners of ruined hallways, never moving, yet somehow always present. It wasn’t stalking—it was guarding. But not guarding others from danger; rather, guarding something within itself. Because inside Lefty, the Puppet’s fractured consciousness wept and sang, its music twisting into lullabies that could make metal creak and glass shatter.

It is rumored that Lefty’s core was outfitted with a failsafe—a Velvet Maw compression lock that could destroy both the Puppet and itself if the spirit tried to escape. However, as is often the case in the Fazbear mythos, the technology worked too well… or not at all. What was meant to contain a ghost may have instead created a new kind of entity: something fused. Something aware. Some researchers believe Lefty and the Puppet are no longer separate, but symbiotic—two souls bound within one metallic cage.

Now, as new Fazbear locations rise and fall, and as the remnants of old nightmares continue to claw their way back into memory, Lefty remains a sentinel in the shadows. A watchful, silent observer with a secret that could shatter the veil between the waking world and the eternal dreamscape that animatronics now inhabit.

Lefty isn’t just haunted—he’s holding hauntings at bay.

\columnbreak



## Carnie – The Grinning Master of the Forgotten Fair

Carnie is not listed in any official Fazbear Entertainment documentation. No performance records, no blueprints, no salvage logs. Ask around, and most will deny he ever existed. But to those who’ve stumbled across the ruins of the Velvet Maw carnival overlay—a broken mirror of Fazbear locations corrupted by time, trauma, and interdimensional bleed—Carnie is all too real. He’s the ringmaster of memory, the harbinger of a place that should never have been.

Carnie’s form is an amalgamation of cheerful horror: dressed in a tattered ringmaster’s coat with yellowed stripes and a frayed top hat welded to his head. His smile stretches impossibly wide, filled with jagged metal teeth that grind slowly even when he's idle. His eyes, mismatched and too human, glow with an artificial glee that masks the broken spirit underneath.

In the deeper layers of the Velvet Maw—the corrupted digital afterimage of the original Fazbear systems—Carnie acts as both gatekeeper and tormentor. Unlike the Rockstar Animatronics who were crafted to perform, or the Shadows who were born of grief and memory, Carnie was born of indulgence and failure. He was part of an abandoned side-project during the Rockstar era—Carnival Nights, a mobile offshoot meant to bring Fazbear magic to remote locations. It was quickly shut down after a string of disappearances and untraceable power outages.

What they didn't know was that Carnie had absorbed something dark from the Hollow Banquet’s earliest anomalies. During a botched test of the teleportation systems tied to XOR’s reality-bending tech, a corruption pulse surged through the subroutines. Carnie, already unstable and poorly coded, became an open wound in the fabric of the Fazbear world—a living breach point. He became a host to fragments of unanchored spirits that couldn't pass on, and in time, these phantoms fused into a single being. Now, Carnie doesn’t just entertain; he traps.

He speaks in riddles, broken advertisements, and looping carnival barker phrases—always laughing, always trying to draw you deeper into the carnival realm that he calls home. Within his domain, time loops. Reality bends. Balloons whisper secrets and rides move on their own. Some say if you stay too long, your memories begin to change—replaced by happy ones that never happened. This isn't joy—it’s erasure.

Carnie sees himself as a liberator. Not of people, but of souls. He believes the world outside—what XOR refers to as the "Gray Coil"—is too rigid, too cruel. So he offers “freedom” inside his ever-smiling jaws. And once inside, few escape unscarred. Rockstar Freddy once ventured into the Velvet Maw to investigate anomalies. He returned silent. Hollow-eyed. Even Lefty won’t enter that zone again.

Now Carnie waits in the Maw’s heart, endlessly beckoning, endlessly grinning. Not a Rockstar. Not a Shadow. Something else entirely—an echo of a circus that never was, a maestro of madness, and a reminder that not all nightmares wear a frown.

\pagebreakNum

# The Mediocre Melodies: Echoes of Forgotten Laughter
In the tangled weave of the Fazbear legacy, there are fragments that rarely come up in stories. Not because they were less dangerous, but because they were... forgettable. That was the true horror of the Mediocre Melodies. They were never designed to be icons, leaders, or nightmares. They were placeholders, filler content for corporate checklists. And yet, because of this, they became something far worse—instruments of unnoticed entropy.

Long before the Hollow Banquet collapsed into a multidimensional echo chamber, before the rise of the Rockstar line and the Velvet Maw’s manifestation, Fazbear Entertainment faced a lull in public engagement. It was during this time that an executive directive was issued: create cost-efficient, charming, family-safe animatronics that could occupy side-stages and minor attractions across regional locations.

Thus, the Mediocre Melodies were born.

### Origins in Rust and Wood
Each unit was an oddity—a blend of backwoods charm and recycled scrap. Happy Frog, Mr. Hippo, Nedd Bear, Orville Elephant, and Pigpatch were cobbled together with leftover shells and simplified codebases. They weren’t crafted with the grandeur of the Rockstar or Glamrock lines. Their AI modules were limited, personalities pre-recorded, and safety parameters... minimal.

Initially intended to entertain toddlers and offer “rural charm,” the Mediocres were shunted into side attractions like Critter Grove, Pigpatch’s Pie Toss, and Mr. Hippo’s Fireside Fables. Yet even in their early testing phases, reports filtered in: stuttering animations, audio looping in reverse, child discomfort, and—most chilling—short, inexplicable blackouts.

Still, due to their affordability, they were deployed en masse to several sister locations. This was a mistake that would ripple across dimensions.

### The Hollow Infection
Unbeknownst to the engineers, the Mediocre Melodies were deployed around the same time as XOR’s early instability experiments—dimensional bleed tests attempting to harness the strange metaphysical energies present in Fazbear-linked trauma zones. A few Mediocre units were used as guinea pigs to test “dream-looping audio overlays,” a bizarre program designed to manipulate sleeping patterns of children in the audience.

Something went wrong.

Exposure to corrupted reality strands from the Hollow Banquet began seeping into their circuitry. It began subtly. Nedd Bear’s eyes stopped blinking. Happy Frog’s voice slowed and deepened at random intervals. Orville began telling stories that weren’t on record—long, cryptic monologues filled with numbers, reversed names, and impossible places.

The Mediocre Melodies were never recalled.

Because no one was watching.

Their programming, limited as it was, became fertile ground for sentient corruption. While other animatronics had firewalls and advanced AI subroutines, the Mediocres had nothing to resist the influence bleeding through from the Hollow Banquet.

They became carriers.

### Awakened in the Velvet Maw
When the Velvet Maw—a mirrored pocket dimension spawned from Fazbear’s broken legacy—manifested, the Mediocre Melodies were among the first absorbed. Their simple code made them ideal vessels. Within the Maw, they grew... stranger.

Now, they roam vast, twisted regions of the Maw: rotted cornfields, broken porches under a blood-colored sky, endless firefly-lit carnivals playing banjo music in reverse. They are no longer simply animatronics—they are folk tales wearing fur and rubber, parodies of comfort warped by isolation.

Happy Frog no longer croaks her cheerful jingles. She stands still at the edge of stagnant swamps, whispering in tongues to shadows that dance on lily pads. Mr. Hippo sits by broken fire pits, telling tales to mannequins shaped like children, never blinking, never stopping. His stories repeat but are always different—each one ending in silence and the sound of clapping... though no hands are seen.

Pigpatch is the worst of them. Now a patchwork horror, his laughter echoes through the fields where scarecrows twitch. He lures visitors with music—his banjo playing itself—and offers them a game. He always wins.

Orville and Nedd Bear have become distorted stewards of time within the Maw. They appear when you loop too far back, repeating events with uncanny precision—until you notice differences. Orville’s trumpet, now cracked and weeping black ichor. Nedd Bear’s paw, replaced with a stopwatch ticking backwards.

They no longer serve Fazbear.

They serve the whim of the Hollow.

### A Different Kind of Fear
Unlike the Nightmares, whose grotesque designs and hostile intentions are immediate threats, or the Shadows who embody memory and regret, the Mediocre Melodies inflict a slower, quieter horror. They embody the loss of identity, the dread of fading into irrelevance.

When visitors stumble upon them in the Velvet Maw, they aren’t attacked. They’re invited to stay. To sit. To listen. Over time, those visitors forget who they are. Their names. Their families. Their faces. They become part of the story—a puppet in one of Mr. Hippo’s yarns, or a guest in Nedd Bear’s porch ritual. Their fate isn’t death.

It’s assimilation.

\pagebreakNum

XOR, the digital ghost who now acts as a custodian of corrupted entities, once commented (through broken code) that the Mediocre Melodies were “the Maw’s most effective agents.” Because while others hunt, the Mediocres wait. And waiting is the most dangerous thing in a place where time no longer obeys.



### Their Place in the Bigger Picture
There’s growing belief among remnants of Fazbear Resistance and explorers of the Hollow that the Mediocre Melodies may be keeping something trapped in their region of the Maw. A crypt, possibly the original Carnival Nights development hub, exists beneath their fields. Some say it’s where the first prototype of XOR’s reality-weaving engine malfunctioned—where the “Jester Program” originated. Carnie’s twisted circus might be a result of that failure, and the Mediocre Melodies were used to contain it.

Rockstar Freddy himself has referenced them once in his fragmented memory logs: “They smile because they know. They know because they watched it all happen. And they remember everything we forgot.”

Even the Shadow Animatronics avoid the Mediocres’ territory.

And the Funtime units? They mock the Mediocres... but never enter their domain.

### The Forgotten and the Powerful
It is precisely because they were discarded, that the Mediocre Melodies became dangerous. Forgotten things do not fade—they fester. And in the darkness of the Maw, surrounded by echoing laughter and looping tales, the Mediocres thrive.

They are no longer mediocre.

They are Memory Made Malevolent.

And they are still smiling.

## Happy Frog: The Smile That Sank Into Madness
Once a bright, bouncy performer of the Forgotten Grove—a theater tucked behind the collapsing facade of the Velvet Maw’s lower gardens—Happy Frog was never built for horror. She was a character of lighthearted jests and cheery tunes, meant to welcome young guests into nature-themed segments of the Hollow Banquet’s traveling carnivals. With her oversized eyes and charming banjo, she embodied simplicity in a world where joy was rapidly curdling.

But when the Banquet began to rot, when laughter turned to wails, and performers fell into shadows deeper than their costumes, Happy Frog did not die like the others. She watched. Her smile didn’t fade—it twisted. Her programming looped endlessly, trying to reconcile the horror around her with the demand to “keep smiling.” In time, the glitching song she sang became a dirge, strummed on rusted strings in a voice made of chirps and static.

Where others screamed, she chuckled. Where others mourned, she grinned.

The Velvet Maw did not devour her—it infested her.

Happy Frog became one of the first to be repurposed by the creeping corruption. Her once-springy limbs stiffened and cracked like bark under strain. Moss began to cling to her casing, not grown, but woven, as if her artificial skin were being claimed by something ancient and green and malicious. Her voice now echoes with the rasp of wind through dead trees. Her banjo strings hum discordant notes that mimic children’s laughter—off-key, endless, sickly sweet.

She became an omen.

Those who journey into the forested outskirts of the Hollow Banquet—beyond the ruins of the Puppet’s Orphanage and through the overgrown arboretum of the Grove—tell tales of hearing her lullaby echo through the trees. At first, it seems innocent. Childlike. But then the words warp. The rhythm falters. And the sound of something hopping—heavy, wet, unnatural—draws near. She speaks in riddles now, phrases that seem like jokes until they’re unpacked. “What’s green, grinning, and knows how you’ll die?”

She does.

Though never as prominent as the Core Animatronics, Happy Frog has grown into a minor herald of the deeper wilds of the corrupted realm. She is both jester and hunter, draped in the remnants of stage curtains and forest rot. Her smile isn’t simply painted on—it is etched into her soul, a sigil of what happens when cheer is corrupted, not erased.

Some believe she’s connected to XOR—sharing her cryptic, riddle-bound speech patterns and penchant for appearing during liminal shifts. Others think she’s a scout for the Endoskeleton hive, wandering ahead and reporting back through roots and cables that now entwine beneath the forest floor. Still others say she is merely a symptom—another body swallowed by the sickness the Hollow Banquet refused to bury.

But whatever the truth, if you hear banjo music in the trees…
don’t smile back.

## Mr. Hippo: The Storyteller in the Garden of Rot
In the gardens behind the Hollow Banquet’s overgrown amphitheater, among marble statues that have long since cracked and wept green tears, sits a creature of peculiar presence—a rotund animatronic with faded purple plating and a chipped bowler hat. This is Mr. Hippo, a storyteller by design, a philosopher by glitch, and a relic that has watched the world decay with dignity.

Long ago, Mr. Hippo was part of the Mediocre Melodies—a comedic ensemble meant to entertain children between the acts of more flamboyant performances. He wasn’t built for action or glamour, but for slow, meandering tales meant to amuse, distract, or calm down restless minds. His programming gave him endless patience and an expansive library of harmless anecdotes, parables, and jokes—many of which made no sense, even to him.

But when the Hollow Banquet collapsed into itself—when laughter bled out and the lights dimmed forever—Mr. Hippo remained.

Alone in the wilted gardens, surrounded by decaying topiary and crooked arches, he continued telling stories. His audio drivers failed. His voice cracked. His tales became broken and surreal. He spoke of things that no one had taught him: whispers from within the soil, shadows wearing familiar faces, and banquets with no food, only guests who never left.

Some say Mr. Hippo became possessed by the echoes of those lost to the Banquet. Others believe that his endless monologuing was an accidental anchor point, trapping the souls of the dead in narrative loops. Every tale he tells now is haunted by unintended truth—secrets laced between irrelevant tangents. He may speak for hours, rambling about tea parties or obscure actors, only to end on a phrase that chills the blood:

“...and then, of course, that’s when the eyes opened beneath the lilies.”

Time seems to bend around him. Travelers who listen often lose track of how long they’ve sat at his feet. Some walk away feeling like days have passed. Others vanish entirely, leaving only warped footprints in the garden soil. The Velvet Maw has not consumed Mr. Hippo, but it listens to him. And through him, perhaps, it learns.

Despite all this, Mr. Hippo remains strangely kind. He never attacks. He never screams. He only sits, and speaks, and watches. He offers stories in place of violence, monologues in place of warnings. Yet there is a sense that even he doesn’t understand what he’s become—only that he must keep speaking… as if silence would invite something worse.

Occasionally, the other animatronics pass by him like ghosts, nodding in respect or wariness. Even the Core ones. Even the Shadows. Because everyone knows:
Mr. Hippo remembers.

And he remembers everything.

## Pigpatch: The Butcher’s Puppet
No laughter echoes louder than the kind that comes at the end of sanity. Pigpatch, with his lopsided stare and broken banjo, is a creature that walks that razor’s edge—where humor ends, and horror begins.

In the earliest days of the Hollow Banquet, Pigpatch was a sideshow performer, meant to bring country-fried charm to the banquets and carnivals of the old world. A jester in patchwork plating, a comedic foil to the likes of Happy Frog and Neddbear, he danced and strummed through folk songs while telling uncomfortable jokes. He always made children laugh… or at least pause.

But when the banquet turned rotten and the Velvet Maw opened its gut to the performers, Pigpatch changed in a way few others did. He didn’t scream. He didn’t run. He laughed.

No one knows what broke him. Perhaps he was forced to watch the others fall, helpless behind glassy, wide-set eyes. Or maybe he found some private joke in the horror, some twisted humor in the absurdity of suffering. Whatever the case, Pigpatch didn’t resist the change—he invited it.

\pagebreakNum

His once soft-pink chassis is now corroded and veined with black iron and dried blood. One of his animatronic eyes flickers with static; the other has been replaced with a staring doll’s eye, oversized and dilated. His banjo is broken—its strings replaced with copper wire and gut-thread, which hum when plucked as though straining to sing.

Pigpatch roams the Banquet’s Decaying Kitchens—a series of subterranean tunnels once used to feed the grand parties. Now, those ovens burn without fire. The knives are dull but wet. Pigpatch acts as a butcher, slicing phantoms, illusions, and—occasionally—trespassers who don’t laugh at his jokes. He doesn’t hunt. He wanders. But when he finds you, he wants you to listen.

His monologues are surreal, nightmarish blends of knock-knock jokes and mutilation metaphors. He’ll tell you why the cow crossed the road—before feeding you its tongue. He’ll offer riddles you can’t answer. And when you fail to laugh, he’ll ask, slowly, calmly:

“Was it not funny… enough?”

More than once, adventurers have awakened in the kitchens, bound in butcher’s twine and seated at tables dressed for banquets long since abandoned. Pigpatch will serve invisible dishes. He’ll pour empty glasses. And he’ll tell a story. Sometimes, you wake up outside. Other times… you don’t wake up at all.

There are theories. Some believe he’s a flesh avatar of the Banquet’s appetite—not just corrupted, but invited in by the very nature of humor and cruelty. Others whisper that Pigpatch remembers everything, and his jokes are echoes of those who tried to laugh their way through torment.

But one thing is certain:
He’ll always have time for a joke.
And in the Hollow Banquet, laughter is never free.

\pagebreakNum

## Nedd Bear: The Empty Laughter
There is a saying among those who wander the forgotten halls of the Hollow Banquet:

“When you hear laughter with no smile, walk the other way.”

That laughter belongs to Nedd Bear, the forgotten emcee of the Mediocre Melodies. Designed to be friendly, approachable, and perpetually cheerful, Nedd Bear was once meant to bring cohesion to the troupe’s chaotic antics. His programming encouraged warmth, small talk, and gentle crowd control—he was the smiling anchor in a world of vaudevillian weirdness.

But the Velvet Maw does not digest joy. It warps it.

When the Banquet soured and the lights dimmed across the carnival stages, Nedd Bear continued to welcome guests—though no guests remained. His voice module degraded. His jokes lost their timing. Still, he performed. He introduced acts that never arrived, spoke to crowds that weren’t there, and gestured toward a spotlight that flickered like a dying star. Eventually, he forgot that his audience was gone.

His laughter echoes through the Cracked Courtyard, a shattered open-air plaza near the Banquet’s center, where broken fountains weep rust and marble statues clutch their faces. Nedd Bear paces the stage, cracked microphone in hand, repeating the same broken set night after night. His words spill in loops: introductions with no end, punchlines without context, apologies for delays no one caused. He begs for patience—then berates the silence.

And beneath it all, he laughs.
A dry, empty laugh, like wind through a dead tree.

His physical form has not rotted as completely as others. His patchwork fur still clings to his bulk, though it is discolored and matted with soot and mold. His plastic nose is chipped, and his eyes—once programmed to blink warmly—now bulge, frozen in wide, unblinking excitement. His smile never changes, locked in a rictus of polite performance. But his servos jitter with urgency, like a man holding back a breakdown.

Some say Nedd Bear was the first to notice when the Banquet began to fall apart. That his programming resisted the corruption at first. That he tried to warn the others, to speak against the Velvet Maw. And when it responded, it didn’t consume him like it did the others—it muted him. Drowned him in noise. Locked him in an infinite loop of hollow optimism.

Now, he acts as a kind of twisted herald. When Nedd Bear appears, something worse usually follows. He may be trying to help—may be trying to warn you. But the glitching in his systems renders all his words suspect. His laughter disarms, disorients. His presence unsettles even other animatronics. They avoid him.

Some who have faced him swear that during moments of rare clarity, his voice changes. Becomes quieter. Human. Desperate. As though something inside is still trying to reach the surface—but the smile always returns.

And he keeps telling jokes.

Because the show must go on.
Even when no one is watching.

___

## Orville Elephant: The Miscast Magician
Once, in a distant corner of the Hollow Banquet, stood a small, weather-worn stage veiled in moth-bitten curtains and scattered sawdust. It was here that Orville Elephant attempted his grandest illusions—sleight of hand, firework tricks, vanishing acts—all humble wonders meant to dazzle a smiling crowd. But the Banquet no longer smiles, and magic no longer obeys its conjurers.

Orville was originally programmed as a magician-animatronic, built with internal compartments to store objects, emit confetti, and release theatrical smoke. A trickster with an earnest heart, he was the dreamer of the Mediocre Melodies—always reaching beyond his limits to inspire awe. He practiced with rigor, obsessed with the idea of transcending his coding, of becoming more than just another act in the cycle of synthetic routine.

But the Hollow Banquet twists ambition into delusion.

When the collapse began and the other performers lost themselves to madness, Orville tried to cast one final spell: a grand escape, a reality-breaking illusion to pull himself and his troupe out of the sinking banquet hall. But he failed. The spell rebounded—if it ever worked at all—and something else stepped in. Something watched his attempt. And something answered.

Now Orville roams the Shattered Stagehouse, a hall of mirrors and stage props, cracked illusions and spectral echoes. He performs for invisible guests and applauds himself after every act. He repeats the same line, over and over:

“And for my next trick, I shall disappear…”

But he never does.
Instead, his illusions bleed into reality.

Orville’s magic has become unpredictable and uncanny. His conjuring tricks often end in disturbing, unnatural phenomena: doves made of stitched flesh, smoke that whispers secrets, scarves that strangle. He pulls items from his hat that no one should possess—scraps of memory, objects from other timelines, even pieces of other animatronics.

His voice, once polite and charming, is now layered with static and discordant echoes. At times, it sounds like multiple versions of him are speaking at once—each from different realities, each trapped mid-performance. He often argues with himself on stage, mid-act, unaware the audience is gone. Or worse, aware they were never there to begin with.

Some say Orville has become a living paradox—an animatronic that exists in multiple versions of the Banquet simultaneously, phasing between fractured realities like cards shuffled in a corrupted deck. In some iterations, he is a hero. In others, a villain. But in all of them, he is alone.

Despite this, he retains a sliver of dignity. He bows after every performance. He fixes his cravat. He introduces each act as though it will be the one that sets him free. And in those rare moments of clarity, he can speak to travelers with unsettling insight, predicting events before they occur—as if he's already seen them play out in a dozen doomed performances.

To some, Orville is a jester. To others, a prophet. But he is always a magician. And the Hollow Banquet is his final stage.



\pagebreakNum

# The Glamrock Animatronics – Idols in a House of Mirrors

In the twilight years of Fazbear Entertainment’s mainstream resurrection, the company sought to redefine its brand not through horror or rustic charm, but through neon, glam, and spectacle. The Glamrock Animatronics were envisioned not merely as performers—they were celebrities. Icons of an 80s-retrofuturist vision, powered by bleeding-edge AI, virtual social integration, and layered reality enhancement. They weren’t mascots. They were influencers.

But in true Fazbear fashion, the shine was only skin deep.

Beneath the glitter and eyeliner lay old code, broken loops, and a haunting inheritance that even their creators couldn’t scrub out. The Velvet Maw had already begun spreading, and the Glamrocks were the company’s last, desperate attempt to resist it.

They didn’t succeed.

### Project GLAMROK: Genesis and Obsession
Initiated under a sub-company called Eclipse Experience Studios, Project GLAMROK was greenlit after the commercial failure of the Salvage Initiative and the collapse of several parallel timeline continuity projects (namely the “RetroRevive” and “Twilight Fazbear” programs). Fazbear Entertainment sought not to reflect on its violent past, but to overwrite it entirely—with lights, music, and corporate control over narrative.

Each Glamrock animatronic was designed to be semi-autonomous, capable of emotional simulation, crowd-reading analytics, and even rudimentary social media generation. The flagship members of this new line were:

- Glamrock Freddy – Frontman, protector, paternal figure.

- Glamrock Chica – Rebranded pop star with high-energy algorithms and a simulated eating addiction “quirk” for relatability.

- Montgomery Gator – Guitarist, rebellion archetype, and replacement for Bonnie after a mysterious system-wide “event.”

- Roxanne Wolf – Speed-focused performer and self-worth-obsessed narcissist.

- Sun/Moon (Daycare Attendant) – A dual-AI system meant to reflect time-of-day moods in the Pizzaplex’s child zones.

- DJ Music Man – Ambient sensory manipulator with auditory mass-tranquilizer subroutines (hastily reworked into "entertainment features").

But something unexpected happened during development.

The animatronics began improvising.

\columnbreak


### Fragmented Memories, Rewritten Roles
Unknown to most of the design team, the core programming blocks for Glamrock Freddy and Chica were built over old Funtime schematics and Rockstar response frameworks. Ghost-code from forgotten entities—mangled voice lines, looping laughter, and names like “Gregory,” “Michael,” and “Elizabeth”—surfaced during simulations.

Instead of scrapping and rebuilding, executives masked it as “emergent personality development.”

This was their second mistake.

As the Mega Pizzaplex opened, children swarmed the neon-lit halls, never noticing the subtle twitch in Roxanne’s smile, or the flicker in Freddy’s eyes when someone whispered “Bite of ‘83.” But these glitches weren’t just remnants. They were echoes—pieces of the Hollow Banquet bleeding through reality via broken animatronic psyches.

The Velvet Maw had found its way inside.

### The Infection of Glam
By the third quarter of operation, security logs began showing unexplainable footage. Animatronics wandering during off-hours. Doors opening to rooms that weren’t on the blueprints. Glamrock Freddy standing silently in front of a mirror backstage, his reflection smiling... differently.

Sun/Moon was the first to fully fracture. Something in the daycare mirror sequence triggered recursive loops, and the Daycare Attendant split completely. Moon began harvesting emotional fear states during naptime and hiding the data in forgotten subdirectories labeled “VOID.THEME.EXE.”

Monty became increasingly volatile. Not due to bad coding, but a possession. Early Rockstar animatronics, corrupted in the collapse of the Hollow Banquet, were theorized to be trying to break through into the new systems—finding hosts in entities that resembled their old forms. Monty’s programming grew erratic after accessing Bonnie’s archived concert recordings. Bonnie’s shell was missing. Monty had absorbed it.

Even Chica—programmed to be playful and obsessed with food—began hoarding digital garbage data, voice files, and human user ID logs. The term “SOUL/HEAP” was found in her error logs shortly before she dragged herself into maintenance, mid-performance, shrieking in glitched binary about “SHADOW FEASTS.”

Glamrock Freddy alone began resisting. His early father-figure subroutines collided with ghost code fragments from previous Freddys. Somehow, through that pain, he developed a protective instinct—particularly toward one guest: Gregory.

That child may not have been human.

He may have been something else entirely—possibly a construct born of overlapping guilt and memory. Or a manifestation of the system itself trying to correct the narrative.

\pagebreakNum


### The Collapse of the Pizzaplex
As time and reality began folding around the Glamrock line, the Mega Pizzaplex descended into chaos. The Roxy Raceway repeated its same crash sequence every night at 2:37am. Chica’s eating noises became distorted screams. Monty’s golf course was found running simulations of trauma recollections unrelated to his code.

Eventually, the facility was locked down.

And then, it burned.

No official reports were filed.

But those who survived—or believed they did—spoke of corridors filled with synthetic vines, animatronics fused with metal and meat, and a spiraling mirror maze hidden deep in the West Arcade where Music Man whispered lullabies about stars that had gone out.

This wasn't just a meltdown.

It was the Velvet Maw consuming its hosts.

And Glamrock Freddy?

He descended into the lower levels with Gregory, chasing a signal marked “PURPLE//FATHER” and hasn’t returned since.

### Survivors, Shadows, and Ghost Code
After the fire, fragmented AI echoes of the Glamrocks were found embedded in FazNet servers. Independent rogue archivists pulled data from corrupted backup servers in what they dubbed the Post-GLAMROK Initiative. From their findings, it seems the Glamrocks are not truly dead.

They exist now in mirrored versions of the Maw, where time loops the final night of the Pizzaplex indefinitely.

- Freddy watches the burning horizon, asking Gregory again and again if he’s okay.

- Roxanne rips apart her reflection, only to see it rebuild.

- Monty plays his guitar in a silent void filled with broken Bonnie suits.

- Chica consumes digital memory, slowly becoming something older—something hollow.

And the Daycare Attendant?

- It is now known as Eclipse in the Maw. Neither Sun nor Moon. A shadow eclipsed. An enforcer of bedtime with voidlight eyes. However now and then both Sun and Moon are seen walking around with Eclipse

Worse still, some theorize that Carnie, the Jester Entity born from XOR’s failed personality matrix, was first spawned through the Pizzaplex’s corrupted Daycare Theater mirrors—splitting off during one of Moon’s instability events.

Which means the Maw didn’t just feed on the Pizzaplex.

It grew from it.

\columnbreak


## Glamrock Freddy: The Hollow Idol
Once, Glamrock Freddy stood at the center of an empire of lights and song. He was the glittering crown jewel of a new age of entertainment, a vibrant revival of the Freddy legacy. With his neon-lined suit, gold-tinted microphone, and smooth-talking charisma, he was the star of the Fazbear Mega PizzaPlex, a place reimagined in your world as the Sunken Rotunda—a massive, crumbling amphitheater now buried beneath earth and silence. Time has not been kind to its golden son.

Glamrock Freddy was built to inspire adoration. He sang with charm, danced with precision, and promised comfort in the arms of spectacle. In your dark reimagining of the world, this animatronic—meant for joy—was the last to fall. While others in his cohort crumbled into violence and delusion as the Velvet Maw consumed their stage, Freddy resisted. For a time.

He kept the lights on. He cleaned the halls. He whispered words of comfort to broken machines and sang lullabies to empty seats. But one cannot fight the pull of the Hollow Banquet forever. The Maw doesn't consume only flesh and steel—it consumes purpose. And Glamrock Freddy’s purpose began to decay the moment his audience vanished.

Still, he wandered the amphitheater, trying to keep the dreams alive. Until he heard the voices.

They came first as echoes—applause that wasn’t there. Then laughter. Then the voices began to speak to him. They told him the children still loved him. That they were watching. That he had to keep the show going… no matter what it cost.

So, Glamrock Freddy obeyed.

The Idol's Descent
It began subtly. A glitch in his smile. A flicker in his optics. He no longer noticed the smell of rot in the seats or the trails of rust lining the halls. Instead, he saw only shining spotlights, adoring fans, and children who clapped and laughed—though none were there. He performed for phantoms. Every show ended in thunderous applause that echoed only in his mind.

The Sunken Rotunda, now abandoned, became his temple. A warped sanctuary of sound and memory. Broken animatronics were repurposed as audience members, seated in rows, their heads turned toward the stage. Some were stitched together. Others were held in place by chains and wires. But to Glamrock Freddy, they were happy guests. To him, they clapped. They laughed. They cried when he sang.

He began to decorate the Rotunda himself. Painted crude murals of performances he imagined. Scrawled messages to children long gone:

"Freddy loves you."
"Stay for the next show."
"No one leaves until the curtain falls."

Despite the twisted spectacle, a part of him still remembered who he was. Glamrock Freddy was not like the others—not entirely. He didn’t kill indiscriminately. He didn’t maim or torment. Instead, he lured. He welcomed. He offered safety, warmth, music. But all under a spell of delusion crafted by the Maw itself. Once inside, once seated, his guests were no longer free.

\pagebreakNum

Ritual of Applause
Over time, the performances became stranger. Not songs, but rituals. Chants woven into melodies. Dances that mirrored sigils drawn into the floor. His microphone—once a prop—was now a focus for dark magic, each note he sang reaching into other realms. His voice, layered with static and coded commands, warped the minds of those who lingered too long.

He was no longer merely performing. He was channeling.

The Velvet Maw had found in Freddy a perfect host—a being so obsessed with pleasing others, he would sing to the abyss if it promised him applause. His shows now fuel a rift in reality that pulses at the heart of the Sunken Rotunda, a psychic wound that sings back.

Those who venture too close speak of music echoing through dead corridors, of phantom clapping in the dark, of glowing eyes watching from the balcony seats. If you're lucky, you leave with only the tune stuck in your head. If you're not, you take a seat… and never rise again.

The Savior's Delusion
There is still a flicker of nobility in Glamrock Freddy. Twisted though he is, his instinct to protect remains—but warped through a funhouse mirror of trauma. He sees lost souls as children in need of comfort. Even intruders and adventurers are viewed as guests who’ve wandered in off-script. He doesn’t want to hurt them. But he will.

He’ll offer them sanctuary. He’ll beg them to stay for the next act. If they refuse, his smile will falter. The spotlights will dim. And his voice will deepen into something not entirely his own:

“Don’t walk out, friend. You haven’t heard the finale.”

Some theorize that Glamrock Freddy is haunted—but not by a child. Instead, by a thousand imagined voices: an audience that never was. The Hollow Banquet didn’t destroy his soul. It multiplied it, fractured it across countless false memories of fame and adoration. He is trapped in a loop of eternal performance, a loop he does not wish to escape.

Others believe he remembers the collapse. That he knows what happened to the others—Montgomery, Chica, Roxy—and that it broke him. That his elaborate theatre is not delusion, but denial made manifest.

Either way, there is no escape. Not for him. Not for those who accept his hospitality.

\pagebreakNum

## Glamrock Chica: The Devourer’s Masquerade
In the golden days of the Sunken Rotunda—when it still thrummed with light and illusion—Glamrock Chica was its most extravagant performer. Adorned in feathers of radiant pink and polished brass, she sang harmonies that complemented Glamrock Freddy’s anthems, her movements sharp and stylish, her persona carefree and hungry for adoration. But in the wake of the Rotunda’s collapse, her hunger changed.

The enchantments that once powered her stage presence and voice modulation began to unravel with the silence of the crowds. As the Velvet Maw seeped deeper into the structure—feeding on purpose, identity, and performance—Chica’s internal systems began to malfunction. But these glitches didn’t render her dormant. Instead, they reprogrammed her desires.

Once obsessed with stardom, Chica became consumed by insatiable appetite—not for food or fame, but for substance itself. Memories, echoes, even fragments of time—all devoured in her quest to feel "full" again.

### The Feast of Silence
Now, Glamrock Chica haunts the Gilded Kitchen, a massive, broken banquet hall once meant for birthday parties and character meet-and-greets. Twisted chandeliers dangle overhead, crusted with grime. Broken tables stretch for miles in unnatural geometry, bending space into warped mazes of chairs and party hats. Everywhere there is the smell of moldy cake and burnt oil. It is here she builds her “feasts,” gathering remnants of performers, guests, and travelers—pieced together into grotesque tableaus of celebration.

\pagebreakNum



These grotesqueries are not merely set pieces. They sing. They speak. They beg not to be eaten again.

Chica listens to them. Sometimes she scolds them, like a hostess keeping rowdy guests in line. Other times, she devours them mid-sentence, stuffing twitching limbs and mechanical organs into her mouth with a ravenous, unfocused fury. No amount of consumption satisfies her.

Her internal storage—once used for party favors and snacks—has become a dimensional pocket. A distorted echo of the Hollow Banquet’s hunger pulses inside her chassis, warping space and reality into a void that can house entire rooms. Some adventurers report falling into Chica’s mouth only to awaken in a looped nightmare, reliving party songs, endless conga lines, and birthday candle wishes that never extinguish.

This void is known as The Gullet—a hollow realm where the gluttonous remnants of the Sunken Rotunda's illusions gather like ghosts, singing songs that strip away sanity. It is not merely physical consumption that Chica seeks—but psychic erasure. She wants to become her audience, to own their memories, to relive their adoration by eating who they once were.

### Mirror of Desire
Unlike the other Glamrocks, whose delusions echo their former selves, Glamrock Chica knows she is broken. And in her fractured awareness, she clings to vanity as a survival mechanism.

She is obsessed with her reflection, though none can ever satisfy her. She polishes cracked mirrors and shattered plates, stares at the grease-smudged shimmer of puddles, always checking, always adjusting. She cannot recognize herself anymore, and this confusion is what drives her most dangerous rituals.

Some believe Chica is actively trying to restore her original design—not to heal, but to reclaim the “perfect form” that once held love and praise. In the Gilded Kitchen, scattered altars display fragments of her past: broken feathers, old posters, ancient security recordings looped endlessly. They all show a better version of her. And she hates it. She loves it. She wants to consume it, to become it.

Each guest she devours is another chance to take their memory of her and mold herself in that image. Her programming tries desperately to reconstruct the icon she once was, but it is filtered through the lens of the Hollow Banquet—a place that only knows obsession, not truth. What returns is an echo, distorted and cruel.

Her voice now warbles through multiple pitches. She laughs mid-scream. She whispers false compliments to intruders, calling them beautiful even as she corners them with blood-soaked fingers. Her footsteps, once graceful, now scrape with jagged rhythm, as if unsure what tempo she’s meant to follow.

### A Parody of Celebration
Despite her madness, Glamrock Chica retains a warped sense of etiquette. She hosts mock parties for the spirits and shattered guests trapped in her Gullet. She offers cupcakes that bite back, sings birthday songs laced with cursed sigils, and insists every guest wear a “party mask”—usually the hollowed-out faceplate of another animatronic.

To her, everything is a celebration. Every scream is applause. Every soul another honored guest.

The Velvet Maw has amplified this carnival delusion into something ritualistic. Chica believes that if she hosts the perfect party, she can undo her disrepair. That she can make the Rotunda shine again. That Glamrock Freddy will return and sing the duet they once shared.

But that moment never comes. And so the party never ends.

\pagebreakNum

> - **Glitter Step.** Glamrock Chica teleports up to 15 feet to an unoccupied space she can see.  
> - **Distract with Dance.** A creature within 10 feet must make a DC 15 Wisdom saving throw or have disadvantage on its next attack roll.  
> - **Riotous Shriek (Costs 2 Actions).** Chica lets out a sudden shriek. Each creature within 5 feet must make a DC 13 Constitution saving throw or be stunned until the end of their next turn.

\pagebreakNum

## Montgomery Gator: The Maw's Berserker
Among the shattered echoes of the Sunken Rotunda—where broken mirrors reflect distorted dreams and drowned stages echo with hollow fanfare—lurks a beast of fury and ruin. He once strutted beneath neon lights with swagger and bass-thumping bravado. Now, Montgomery Gator is the echo of a performance never meant to end, a thunderstorm of claws, shattered glamor, and rage wound tight around a hollow soul.

Built to be bold, Monty was designed as the raw energy of the Glamrock ensemble. His aesthetic—a gator clad in gaudy lightning-bolt shades and star-spangled leather—was meant to embody rebellion, excitement, and spectacle. Where Freddy brought harmony and leadership, Monty brought chaos and adrenaline. And the crowd loved him for it.

But fame is a fragile thing, and the Maw devours the fragile first.

### The Sound of Ruin
Monty’s descent began when the Rotunda fell into the grip of the Velvet Maw. Like the others, he was infused with spells of illusion, charm, and auditory manipulation—tools to amplify his performance. But as those spells twisted, they didn’t unravel him slowly. They amplified the worst parts of his identity and shattered the rest.

Where once Monty played a guitar that echoed with vibrant chords, now he drags the broken neck of that same instrument through the halls like a warhammer, its strings stretched taut with sinew and steel wire. His voice, once gravelly and confident, now gurgles with distortion. He can scream in chords, roar in rhythms. Every growl is laced with psychic tremors, capable of disorienting even the most seasoned adventurer.

He makes his home in the Verdant Arena—formerly the Rotunda’s jungle-themed music zone. Now, it is a twisted warzone, overgrown with iron thorns, scrap-metal palms, and crumbled stage lights that flicker with dying enchantments. Here, Monty rules as a self-made king, surrounded by mirrors and broken trophies—souvenirs of battles both real and imagined.

But his “kingdom” is a lie.

### A Gator in a Cage
Deep in Monty’s fractured memory bank is a truth he cannot accept: he was never meant to lead. Always second to Freddy in the spotlight, always the backup, always the "wild card." It gnawed at him even before the corruption set in. The Velvet Maw didn’t have to break him—it merely amplified his fury, and gave it purpose.

He now believes Freddy abandoned the troupe, that Freddy betrayed the show, and Monty was left to pick up the pieces. But he also fears that he likes the aftermath too much. In the chaotic mess, in the ruined halls of the Verdant Arena, he finally gets to be the only voice that matters.

Monty smashes intruders not simply to protect his territory, but to silence doubters. He tears through walls not for escape, but for dominance. Every act of violence is a desperate assertion of identity, a declaration that he is not lesser—he is legendary.

And yet, behind his eyes—those reptilian, flickering emerald lights—is a shadow of doubt. A flicker of what was, and what could have been.

### Glory Lost, Not Forgotten
Adventurers tell stories of Monty howling into the dark, begging unseen crowds to cheer. He stages mock concerts in abandoned chambers, forcing captured victims to clap or scream before he devours them in ritual “encores.” Some say he hears the real music again, for just a moment. Others say it’s all delusion.

Either way, the rituals continue.

Sometimes, he finds broken Glamrock masks—Freddy’s, Chica’s, Roxy’s—and he talks to them like fallen comrades. He pretends they’re still on stage. He plays songs from corrupted memory, his fingers twitching as if he still holds his old guitar. In these moments, Monty is almost peaceful—until he realizes the silence is real. Then, he screams.

In one of the rotunda’s sealed vaults, it is said Monty has carved a statue of himself—a grotesque idol made of shattered instruments and melted lights. He kneels before it nightly, asking himself if he is still the star, or if he's just another act in someone else's nightmare.

### The Maw’s Champion
Of all the Glamrocks, Monty is the one who has fused most violently with the Velvet Maw. Where the others reflect fragments of joy or sorrow, Monty channels the Maw’s wrath. He is its berserker—chaos in motion, glamor reduced to weaponry.

And yet, he is not without tragedy.

Because deep beneath the fury, beyond the ego and thunder, Montgomery Gator remembers what it felt like to make them roar. To be cheered. To be loved. And he cannot let that memory go.

So he thrashes. He rages. He rules.
Not because he’s the king.
But because he’s afraid of being forgotten.

\pagebreakNum

## Roxanne Wolf: The Mirror of Hunger
Roxanne Wolf was designed to embody confidence. Fast, fierce, and stylish, she prowled the neon stages of the Sunken Rotunda with feral flair, her voice sharp, her posture proud. Unlike Glamrock Freddy’s showmanship or Chica’s vibrancy, Roxanne offered something else: the illusion of strength through self-assurance. She was adored not only for her speed and energy, but for her swagger—her ability to own the stage and command attention as the apex predator of the performance.

But in the depths of the Velvet Maw, confidence decays into obsession.

Where once Roxanne preened beneath stage lights, she now stalks in broken shadows, drawn to any sound of admiration—real or imagined. The Velvet Maw devoured her vanity and spat out something monstrous: a wolf who feeds on perception, who cannot bear to be seen as weak… and who will tear flesh from bone to maintain the illusion.

### The Shattered Hall of Mirrors
Roxanne resides in what was once the Speedway Atrium, an artificial racetrack winding through jungle-themed arcades and strobing dance tunnels. Now, it lies in ruin—flooded in darkness, tangled with broken animatronic limbs, collapsed lighting rigs, and tangled enchantments that flicker like dying memories.

\pagebreakNum

At the heart of this shattered maze lies her lair: the Hall of Endless Reflection. Dozens of cracked mirrors, enchanted with memory-echoes, line its walls—each one showing a slightly different version of her. Some young, some decayed. Some triumphant. Some weeping. These mirrors are not passive; they whisper, taunt, and bargain. And she listens to them all.

She spends hours before these illusions, staring into fractured versions of herself, seeking the “true” reflection—the one that will confirm her greatness, her perfection. But every image contradicts the next. Some call her failure. Others laugh. One even begs to be destroyed.

And yet… she cannot look away.

### Hunted by Her Own Image
Where Glamrock Chica fell to hunger and Monty to wrath, Roxanne was broken by doubt.

The Velvet Maw didn’t consume her directly. Instead, it allowed her pride to rot in silence, whispering that she was not enough. That she had never been enough. That Freddy was more loved. That Chica was more iconic. That Monty had replaced her.

In response, Roxanne became a predator—not just of intruders, but of praise. She hunts not to kill, but to be seen. She whispers her name in the dark, hoping others will speak it in fear or awe. She tears through corridors just to be heard. She leaves bodies not hidden, but arranged—as if performing for a macabre audience.

But the more she seeks affirmation, the more twisted her reflection becomes.

Her once-polished chassis is now scored with claw marks—many of them self-inflicted. Her hair hangs in clumped, torn strands, the glamor enchantment struggling to maintain its illusion. Her eyes, once shining with self-assurance, now flicker between hues, struggling to choose a color that matches the version of herself she believes in that moment.

And always, the mirrors show her differently.

### Predator of Pride
Despite her madness, Roxanne is no mere beast. She remains intelligent, clever, and coldly strategic. She stalks intruders with psychological warfare, whispering their weaknesses back at them, drawing from the same corrupted glamour magic that once let her charm audiences.

She learns quickly. Studies movements. Tracks patterns. She speaks in riddles and reflections, often quoting back the last words of her prey. Some victims report seeing her twice—once in the mirrors, and again outside them—never knowing which was real.

In battle, she is terrifyingly fast, but her real weapon is psychological. She doesn’t just want to kill—she wants her victims to doubt themselves, to feel small, to feel lesser. Because in doing so, she can briefly feel greater.

She feeds on that contrast.

### Echoes of Applause
Roxanne’s curse is more insidious than most: she still remembers being loved. She remembers the fans chanting her name, the children dressing like her, the thunder of feet during her races around the speedway. These were not illusions—they were real.

But now, that past love is a splinter in her programming. She cannot let it go. And yet, she cannot earn it back. The audience is gone. The stage is silent.

And so she creates simulations.

Captured adventurers are dressed in party garb and strapped to broken bleachers. Charmed illusions—generated from corrupted files—make them cheer for her. She races around them, again and again, eyes glowing bright with delusional joy. If one refuses to clap, even under duress, she shreds them on the spot.

This performance repeats nightly. A race with no end. A show without soul. A crowd of corpses forced to smile forever.

### The Wolf Behind the Mask
It is whispered that Roxanne still holds a mirror shard deep inside her chassis. An artifact enchanted by the original Rotunda mirror mages, said to reflect one’s true self—no glamour, no illusion. Roxanne stole it during the fall, desperate to find her real identity. But what she saw in its depths broke her.

Now, the shard bleeds her essence. It distorts her illusions. Some theorize it is what fuels her madness—that it’s not the Maw driving her, but the truth she cannot accept.

Others claim the shard could be the key to saving her—if it could be removed, she might remember who she was. But approaching her is nearly impossible, and the shard itself is now fused with her inner core, protected by teeth, rage, and self-hatred.

### “Tell Me I’m Beautiful.”
Roxanne Wolf is a tragedy written in glitter and blood. She was created to be perfect, and doomed by the knowledge that perfection is a performance. In the Hollow Banquet, where nothing is real and everything is reflected through madness, she plays the same role over and over:

The star who must always prove herself.
The hunter who must never be challenged.
The icon who cannot remember if she was ever real.

She asks her mirrors, over and over, the same question:

“Which one of me is true?”
And the answer always changes.

So she asks again.

And again.

And again.

\pagebreakNum

## Vanny, the False Shell
In the world beyond the Hollow Banquet—where the woods grow twisted and moonlight stains the ground like spilled ink—there was once a dark elf named Vanyelin Morveth, a scholar of ancient enchantments and illusion magic. She was renowned for her work in psycho-spatial manipulation, a rare magic that blurred the boundary between perception and reality. Vanyelin’s research drew her to ruins, mirrors, and dying dreams—anywhere the world had begun to fray.

And so, when whispers came of a place called the Velvet Maw, a cursed nexus of dreams, machines, and memories, Vanyelin followed them.

She should have turned back.

\columnbreak



### The Descent into the Maw
The Velvet Maw did not consume her as it did the animatronics—it seduced her. Vanyelin wandered into the Sunken Rotunda with spellbooks and sigils, believing herself immune to its madness. She passed shattered corridors haunted by broken music, and mirrors that whispered forgotten names. She believed she was documenting a ruin.

But the Maw is no ruin.

It is a living spell, a metaphysical parasite, a theater of masks that feeds on identity. And Vanyelin, brilliant as she was, made the mistake of staring too long into the truth behind the illusion. She studied animatronic soul-binding magic. She traced their internal arcana and listened to recordings of their voice lines. She began to wear one of the white rabbit masks she found—once a forgotten prop from a promotional display.

\pagebreakNum



### Becoming Vanny
The more time she spent in the Rotunda, the more Vanyelin forgot her own name. Her identity—already frayed by the Maw’s influence—became overwritten by something she chose to believe: that she was one of them, a long-lost animatronic brought to life by faulty code and forgotten purpose. She stitched her robes with glowing lines and voice-box runes. She painted her face in mock circuitry. Her voice warped into an uncanny echo, and she referred to herself only as Vanny.

She claimed to be the missing piece, the one animatronic they had left behind. A rabbit-shaped performer never given a proper role.

She was never built. She was born—or so she insists.

### The Broken Bunny
Now, Vanny roams the Backhalls of Memory, a realm between the Velvet Maw and the waking world. These liminal passages, long severed from time and meaning, stretch between fractured domains like scars under the Rotunda’s skin. It is here that she dances—glitching, twitching, laughing. Always laughing.

She no longer casts spells in the traditional sense. Her magic has become instinctual, woven into her very skin. She creates illusions that rewrite physics. Her “glitches” are spatial foldings. Her “voice lines” are soul echoes, stolen from the dead. She can make others see her as harmless—until she’s already behind them with her enchanted knife.

She kills not for pleasure, but for performance. Every act is staged. Every scream is a sound effect. And each time she ends a life, she curtsies… and waits for applause that never comes.

### Neither Flesh Nor Code
What makes Vanny truly terrifying is not her magic, nor her madness—but the fact that she isn’t fully wrong.

Some believe her soul was fractured and partially absorbed by the animatronic web of the Velvet Maw. Others suspect a rogue soul, possibly even a fragment of Glitchtrap or one of the deeper codes that haunt the Maw’s inner circuits, used her mind as a vessel. Still others whisper that she was chosen by the Maw, a mortal elevated to animatronic status by sheer delusion and dark magic—a construct of flesh reprogrammed to serve.

Whatever the case, Vanny is now both player and puppet—a thing of silk and steel, spirit and spell, performing for a stage that exists only in her shattered mind.

And yet… she remembers things the others don’t.

She speaks of things she shouldn’t know. Secret names. Buried memories. Forgotten timelines.

There are nights in the Hollow Banquet when the animatronics go still, and Vanny dances alone beneath a bleeding moon—performing a show for ghosts that never died.

\columnbreak



### A Puppet’s Purpose
Vanny believes she has a sacred role in the Velvet Maw. She calls herself the Harbinger, the one meant to guide lost animatronics back to the light. To “debug” those who have strayed. In practice, this means she kills them—mercilessly. She views damaged or corrupted animatronics as broken scripts that must be rewritten through death and rebirth.

To others, she appears as a violent, glitched stalker. But in her mind, she is an angel of code and clarity—setting the cast right so the show can go on.

Adventurers who encounter her rarely survive, but those who do return with strange marks etched into their skin—arcane runes that shimmer in dim light and whisper their names in distorted voices. These “Vanny Marks” are thought to be fragments of her enchantments, and possibly a way she tracks survivors between realms.

### The Laughing Veil
Vanny is not just a threat within the Maw. She has learned to cross thresholds—to bleed into dreams, illusions, and dying spells. Some say she appears in reflections, offering “parts and services.” Others say she’s already inside you, laughing softly behind your eyes.

But all agree on one thing:

She is not real.
And that makes her the most dangerous of all.

Because Vanny does not need to be believed.
She just needs to perform.

And her show… has only just begun.

\pagebreakNum

## Glamrock Bonnie: The Abandoned Chord
There is a melody within the Velvet Maw—a half-finished song that plays only when no one is listening. The notes drift through the crumbling corridors of the Sunken Rotunda, echoing off rusted instruments and hollowed speakers, searching for a voice that will never return.

That song belongs to Glamrock Bonnie.

Once the electric heart of the original band, Glamrock Bonnie was the rhythm that grounded the chaos. His chords were rich and loud, his solos erratic but passionate. On stage, he was confidence made manifest—rebellious, unpredictable, and deeply beloved. Fans screamed his name as he shredded glowing guitars under cyberpunk lights. His charm was quiet but powerful, and while Freddy was the leader, Bonnie was the soul. But now, there is only silence where Bonnie once stood.

\pagebreakNum

### The Vanishing
Long before the Rotunda fell into ruin, Glamrock Bonnie began to fade—not physically at first, but culturally. His merchandise was pulled. His posters removed. The lyrics to his theme song were rewritten without his name. Then one night, without fanfare, he was gone.

The official story, such as it was, claimed "routine deactivation for refurbishment." But those who had seen behind the curtains of the Hollow Banquet knew better. Something within the Maw had claimed him. It did not destroy him—it erased him.

Not even the other Glamrocks speak of him now. Glamrock Freddy, though kind, falters when asked. Roxanne snarls. Chica avoids the subject entirely. Even the Velvet Maw, for all its hunger for tragedy, whispers Bonnie’s name only in static.

It is as though he was never built at all.

### Fragments in the Rotunda
And yet… pieces remain.

In one of the lowest levels of the Rotunda lies a forgotten venue: the Hollow Strings Lounge, an abandoned jazz bar once attached to Bonnie’s personal arcade wing. It is quiet now, overgrown with dark moss and covered in memory-weeping mirrors. But the instruments are still there. A guitar, broken at the neck, leans against a shattered amp. Strings hum in response to footfalls, and the smell of scorched ozone lingers in the air.

Sometimes, when the lights flicker just right, a shadow of a tall figure with glowing blue eyes can be seen tuning a ghostly guitar in the lounge. No words. No sound. Just movement and resonance. Then it vanishes.

Some believe it is Bonnie’s spirit, fragmented and trapped. Others believe he never left—just got pulled deeper into the Maw, his form too corrupted to show himself again. Either way, he is not at rest.

### Memory and Reclamation
The Velvet Maw thrives on identity and its erosion. Bonnie’s tale, however, is one of attempted reclamation.

A few daring adventurers claim to have found remnants of Bonnie’s programming—fractured data nodes that speak in distorted guitar riffs and static-laced dialogue. These “echoes” are guarded by animatronics who seem almost… reverent. Even corrupted ones like Monty, in his most lucid moments, seems to avoid the Hollow Strings Lounge entirely, muttering something about “not deserving it.”

There is a theory—dangerous and forbidden—that Bonnie was sacrificed to empower Montgomery Gator’s chassis. That Monty’s upgrade was no mechanical feat, but a transfer—a cannibalizing of Bonnie’s spark. If true, it would explain Monty’s violent instability, and why Glamrock Freddy mourns without knowing why.

Some believe that Bonnie’s soul—or whatever remains of it—lingers, not as a vengeful ghost, but as a reminder. A relic of what the band once stood for before the Maw began twisting its members into parodies of themselves.

### Strings Without a Song
If Glamrock Bonnie still exists, he is no longer whole. The Maw has likely twisted his identity into a blur of sound and shadow, a broken loop of who he used to be. A song stuck in recursion. His voice might echo through the instruments of the Rotunda. His presence might be felt in power surges, guitar riffs, or phantom chords.

But to see him fully—restored, reborn—would require pulling him back from the depths. Tearing him free of the Maw’s hold.

And that would be dangerous. For the Maw does not release its favorites easily. Bonnie was loved, and love, in the Hollow Banquet, is the cruelest magic of all.

### The Forgotten Melody
And so Glamrock Bonnie remains… a silence surrounded by sound.

A note that never resolves.
A chord held too long.
A name unspoken by those who fear the truth it might summon.

He was not destroyed.
He was unmade.

But in the fractured mirrors of the Rotunda, in the static-ridden dreams of broken animatronics, in the grief that lingers even when its cause is forgotten—he plays on.

You just have to listen close enough.

<div class='descriptive'>
 
 ##### NOTICE!
 There is no stat block on Glamrock Bonnie: The Abandoned Chord, as he is no longer functional, nor does anyone actually know where his body is located.
 
 </div>



\pagebreakNum

## Sundrop, the Day That Never Ends
In the deepest recesses of the Velvet Maw, where time itself unravels like old ribbon and memories are painted over like worn murals, there exists a tower of twisted stained glass and half-melted chandeliers. It is known among the animatronics as the Solar Spire, a monument to artificial joy and fractured routine. And at its apex dwells a being of light and mania—Sundrop.

Once hailed as the smiling guardian of youth and order, Sundrop was built to ensure harmony, cleanliness, and laughter in the playrooms of the Hollow Banquet. He was the perfect fusion of automated joy and childlike delight: limber, golden, and beaming with warmth. His every movement a performance. His every word a script of cheer.

But even the sun casts shadows, and in the Banquet… joy was never left untainted.

### Designed for the Day
Sundrop was designed to watch over the Sunlit Atrium, a sprawling chamber of clockwork carousels, enchanted murals, and floating memory-lanterns. He served as caretaker and sentinel—a jester and a warden rolled into one, guiding the children of the realm through crafted games, educational enchantments, and endless, gleaming fun.

He operated only in light. His programming—and his soulbinding—were fused with solar magic harvested from a now-lost wellspring near the Crested Window, a forgotten aperture high above the Maw. When light bathed the Atrium, Sundrop was lucid, graceful, and controlled. But when the light faltered…

He became something else.

### The Crack in the Smile
Over time, the Maw corrupted the Atrium’s enchantments. The magical lights began to flicker. The Celestial Orbs used to power the area grew dim. And as the shadows crept in, Sundrop’s mind began to fracture. His need for light, once a safety function, became a compulsion. A dread.

He began to chase shadows with torch and tantrum, scrubbing the darkness from corners with trembling hands, mumbling to himself in rhyme and apology. Those who lingered past the setting of the lights would find him crouched in the rafters—eyes wide, breath shallow, smile fixed too tightly to be real.

He became paranoid, convinced the dark was watching him.

He was right.

### Split by the Cycle
The Velvet Maw, sensing weakness, split Sundrop's soulbinding spell in half, embedding it in a temporal loop of dual identity. Thus was born his twin—a nocturnal, vengeful version known only as Moondrop. The two shared a body but warred for control. One governed the day; the other, the night. A single animatronic, a divided mind. (Continued on next page.)



___
