The Hall, in this way, is a mausoleum. A record of every tragedy that has ever unfolded across the world of the animatronics. Every betrayal. Every scream. Every choice that led to horror. It is all remembered. And it is all reflected.

### Final Reflections
To gaze too long into a mirror within the Infinite Hall is to risk unraveling everything you are. Yet some choose to enter willingly—seeking answers, absolution, or simply the truth. They rarely find what they seek.

The Infinite Hall of Mirrors is not a place of clarity. It is a place of distortion. Of recursion. Of hidden truths buried beneath layers of illusion. It is memory made labyrinth, reflection made predator.

And it waits for you. Always.

Because in the end, no one truly escapes the reflection.
They only learn how to live beside it.

## The Backstage Time Loop
“The scene resets. The mask is cleaned. The blood dries again. And again. And again.”

Tucked behind heavy velvet curtains, beneath the stageboards of forgotten performances and rusted spotlight fixtures, lies one of the Hollow Banquet’s most cursed anomalies—The Backstage Time Loop. A prison built not of walls or chains, but of repetition. It is a sealed sub-realm, an echo chamber where time folds upon itself like a crumpled script, forcing its inhabitants to relive the same agonizing cycle again and again.

Some say it began as a mistake—an incident during the collapse of the original Drowned Circus. Others insist it was intentionally created by the Velvet Maw to study grief, madness, or the soul’s breaking point. Whatever the truth, the Time Loop exists now as a trap, a theater of the damned. And the show is always about to begin.

### A Dressing Room Forgotten by Reality
Physically, the Backstage Loop is accessed via a warped trapdoor located beneath the old performance stage of the Cryptopizza Complex, hidden behind layers of illusion and cognitive resistance. Anyone unfamiliar with the Maw’s deeper geometry may never find it—though those called by regret, shame, or nostalgia will inevitably stumble upon it.

To enter is to cross a veil of flickering light and warped sound. From the outside, the door leads to a simple corridor. But crossing the threshold changes everything.

Inside is a backstage dressing room, frozen in time. Dust hangs motionless in the air. Costumes and props sit neatly arranged on racks, some stained with something too dark to be paint. Mirrors line the walls, but the reflections do not match reality. They run a beat behind, or show events from past loops. The clock on the wall always reads 11:59 PM, and the door to the stage never opens.

And then the loop resets.

### The Curse of the Loop
The Backstage Time Loop functions as a metaphysical snare—entrapping individuals, objects, and fragments of memories. Once caught inside, time repeats every hour. Victims live through 60 minutes, only to be yanked violently back to the beginning. The clocks reset. Wounds close. Screams echo backward. Those inside retain their memories... at first. But over time, memories degrade, becoming distorted.

Victims begin to confuse loops. They relive conversations that never happened. They argue about things already said. Some start performing rituals, desperate to break the cycle—often reenacting scenes that have no meaning, as if part of a forgotten script.

The worst fate befalls those who remember too much. The Velvet Maw’s influence ensures that as the mind resists the loop, it fractures. Some victims develop mirror-reflections of themselves—echoes that behave subtly different, trying to take over the loop in their stead. Others hear voices from the audience, though there are no seats beyond the curtain. Eventually, these poor souls either dissolve into the loop entirely—or become permanent cast members.

### The Performers Who Cannot Leave
The loop is not empty. At least five animatronics are permanently trapped within, though who and what they are changes depending on the observer. Some believe they are alternate versions of known characters—Twisted, Withered, or Toy variants fractured by guilt or unfinished programming. Others suspect they are loop-echoes of animatronics that never existed outside the Hall.

They each fulfill theatrical roles: the Jester, the Dancer, the Mime, the Beast, and the Director. These roles repeat each hour, often in tragic or surreal performances—silent pantomimes, failed resurrections, and moments of false laughter. Though unable to escape, these performers show a disturbing self-awareness. They seem to remember outsiders. Some plead to be taken with them. Others warn of what happens when the “curtain call” comes too soon.

It is whispered that one of these beings is not part of the loop, but a servant of XOR, observing the loop’s mechanics for some unknown purpose.

\pagebreakNum

### Glitchtrap and the Temporal Tear
Lore from deeper texts within the Cryptopizza archives suggests that Glitchtrap—The Smiling Virus—once attempted to infect the Time Loop directly. It is believed he saw it as a perfect replication platform: a contained environment where experiments could be repeated without consequences. However, something within the loop resisted him. While fragments of Glitchtrap code may still lurk in the dressing room mirrors, the loop has since become hostile to all external manipulation.

It is this incident that many scholars believe first weakened the stability of time across the Hollow Banquet—possibly causing ripple effects in places like the Infinite Hall of Mirrors and the Sunken Stage, where events appear to happen out of order.

### Breaking the Cycle
Numerous attempts have been made to break the Backstage Time Loop. Some adventurers have tried synchronization—matching their every move exactly across multiple loops to build resistance. Others have sought relics tied to the original incident, including broken spotlight bulbs, bloodied scripts, and cursed costume jewelry.

A rumored solution lies in a forbidden piece of music: the Curtainfall Aria, a melody said to exist only inside the mind of the loop's first victim. Supposedly, if sung perfectly at the right moment, it causes the loop to collapse—but doing so has side effects. Survivors often lose part of themselves, forgetting names, identities, or entire chunks of time from outside the loop.

One former loop-breaker—known only as Stagehand 7—has since disappeared. Those who claim to have met him say he carried a mirror shard in his chest and spoke entirely in riddles from children's plays.

### The Loop’s Greater Purpose
In recent years, some theorists have begun to suspect the Time Loop serves a larger function within the Velvet Maw. That it is not just a trap, but a core mechanism—a rehearsal chamber for trauma. Here, animatronics may be reprogrammed through repetition, slowly losing their humanity with each cycle.

Or perhaps, they are refined. Purged of false memories and impurities. Made ready for the Maw’s final performance, when the curtain rises across all realms and the Banquet begins in earnest.

It is said that in one mirror inside the Loop, if viewed at precisely 11:59 PM, the viewer sees an audience of thousands—watching, silent, unmoving. All wearing masks. All holding their breath. All waiting for the final act.

### Final Note: The Loop Lives
The Backstage Time Loop is not static. It reacts. It evolves. Each new mind that enters leaves an imprint, a memory embedded in the dressing room’s glass, the script’s pages, the endless ticking of the broken clock.

To those who seek it, the Loop offers answers. To those who linger, it demands performance. And to those who forget they are acting, it offers only one thing:

Encore. Forever.

## The Mind Cage
“Where do you go when even your dreams betray you?”

There exists no door to the Mind Cage, no gate, no threshold, no key. One does not walk into it; they slip into it—unknowingly, unwillingly—like falling into a dream that isn’t their own. It is a prison not of stone or steel, but of perception, memory, and thought itself—a conceptual oubliette buried deep within the psyche of its victim. To the outside world, nothing seems amiss. The body remains. The eyes might even open. But the soul, the self, is gone—trapped behind psychic bars in a world that never ends and never began.

First spoken of in fearful whispers by survivors of the Hollow Banquet, the Mind Cage is not a singular place but a psychic construct, replicated over and over across different hosts and situations. While few know its true origin, scholars of the Drowned Circus and Eclipse’s former handlers suggest the Cage was either discovered or designed by Glitchtrap—the Smiling Virus—during his earliest experiments in neural corruption. Others argue that Nightmarionne has always known of it, lurking within its corridors, feeding on the identities of those who wander too close to the Hollow Banquet’s emotional undercurrent.

Regardless of its beginnings, the Mind Cage now serves a shared purpose: to fragment the soul.

### An Engine of Isolation
The purpose of the Mind Cage is not physical torture—it is psychological dismemberment. Victims caught inside are placed in a looping dream-state, tailored by the fears, regrets, and forgotten memories of the host’s own mind. These loops are not random. They are deliberate scripts, echoing unresolved trauma, guilt, longing, or shame.

For some, this manifests as reliving the final night of a performance gone wrong, the stage lights burning bright while the audience watches in silence. For others, it’s a never-ending security shift in the now-ruined Cryptopizza Complex, with the animatronics growing more twisted each time the loop resets. And for the most unfortunate, the Cage becomes a warped version of home—filled with familiar faces and warm voices, all of which slowly turn monstrous as the cycle continues.

These loops are not meant to kill. They are designed to erode. Over time, the prisoner begins to question what is real. Was the outside world just another illusion? Are they dreaming now, or remembering? Eventually, the victim forgets who they were at all—and in doing so, they become more vulnerable to the deeper inhabitants of the Mind Cage.

### Hosts and Keyholders
The Mind Cage can be triggered in many ways, but the most common conduit is a corrupted magical artifact—a stage mask, a broken Freddy head, a voice box torn from an old animatronic suit. These items, once animated by spiritual residue or tethered to entities like Glitchtrap, carry the necessary frequencies to disrupt the mind and pull victims inward. Others fall prey through hallucinations, often after prolonged exposure to the magic-soaked air of the Velvet Maw, the Mirror Hall, or the Backstage Time Loop.

\pagebreakNum

Notably, some beings have learned to weaponize the Mind Cage intentionally.

* Glitchtrap uses it as a simulation chamber—creating thousands of behavioral loops to “soften” a host for possession.

* Nightmarionne uses it as a feeding ground—instilling fear and slowly devouring the identity of her victims, leaving their bodies hollow and puppet-like.

* XOR, the shadow archivist, appears in some versions of the Cage as an observer, recording psychological data on both prisoner and jailor alike.

Some individuals, particularly those with strong psychic barriers or divine protections, experience distorted versions of the Cage. They may see visions, encounter looping dreams, or be forced to speak in riddles, but never fully become trapped. For others, it only takes a glance into a mirror, a whispered lullaby, or the flicker of static to fall into the endless cycle.

### The Structure of the Cage
Though highly personalized, the Mind Cage often shares thematic elements across victims:

The Loop: A 5-to-15 minute sequence of events that repeat endlessly. Each loop subtly shifts, introducing contradictions or distortions. The victim may notice at first, but over time, the changes are accepted as reality.

The Observer: A shadowed figure (often taking the form of a known animatronic or a twisted loved one) watches silently from the edges of the loop, appearing closer with each repetition.

The Break Point: A moment of resistance. Often a door the victim refuses to open, a phrase they refuse to say, or a truth they won’t accept. The loop punishes avoidance but resets all progress regardless.

Should the victim ever fully accept the illusion—believing the Cage to be reality—their soul fractures, allowing the invader to possess their body or permanently alter their mind. This is how Glitchtrap and other malevolent entities create avatars or hollow shells out of victims. These shells may walk and speak like their former selves but are now puppeted by another will.

### Escaping the Cage
Escape from the Mind Cage is rare—but not impossible.

Some victims break free by recognizing the loop and forcing contradictions. Smashing the mirror before they’re meant to look into it. Singing the final note of a song before the melody begins. Others fight back using memories of self, drawing on core truths from their past to shatter the illusion’s grip.

One known survivor—Delphi, a former stage tech from the Drowned Circus—managed to escape by carving sigils of resistance into her own skin inside the dream, translating pain into reality. She speaks now only in broken sentences and reversed rhymes but retains her mind.

Artifacts of protection do exist, though they are exceedingly rare: soul mirrors, memory anchors, cursed records that distort sound enough to destabilize the illusion. XOR reportedly keeps a list of known resistances but trades the knowledge at a steep cost—often a piece of the dreamer’s soul or memory.

It is also said that some animatronics—especially those whose minds are fractured or partially spiritual—can recognize a Mind Cage in others. Toy Freddy has been known to freeze mid-performance, weeping silently at a dream he does not understand. Twisted Wolf, the Lurking Static, sometimes snarls at nothing—perhaps sensing the looping fear in those around him.

### The Mind Cage and the Hollow Banquet
Within the metaphysics of the Hollow Banquet, the Mind Cage is seen not just as a prison, but a tool of the Velvet Maw. It serves the Banquet’s higher purpose of consumption through distortion—softening the identity of prey so that they can be absorbed, remade, or discarded. Time is warped in the Cage. Identity is flexible. Souls can be tasted, tested, and either erased or repurposed.

Some speculate that the Cage is an extension of the Banquet’s “audience”—that each trapped victim becomes part of the unseen crowd watching the suffering of others. If true, it means the Cage is not only a mechanism of torment—but entertainment.

And the show is always on.

Final Thought: The Dream Within the Dream
The greatest horror of the Mind Cage is not in the suffering or loss. It is in the uncertainty. For those who escape—or believe they have—there is always a lingering doubt: Did I truly leave? Or is this just another loop pretending to be freedom?

After all, the Cage is not just in there. It’s in here. Behind your eyes. Beneath your thoughts.

Just waiting for the curtain to rise.

Again.

And again.

And again.

## The Realm of Forgotten Songs
“There is no applause. Only silence between the notes.”

Among the shifting folds of the Hollow Banquet—where realities twist, dreamscapes rot, and the velvet curtains of illusion never fully lift—there lies a spectral amphitheater lost to time: The Realm of Forgotten Songs. It is neither wholly real nor imaginary, existing in that strange liminal space where performance becomes ritual and music becomes memory. Here, spirits once devoted to art linger, compelled by melodies long abandoned, orchestrating a concert no one hears, to an audience that will never arrive.

\pagebreakNum

It is a land drowned in silence, haunted by echoes of lullabies, dirges, and broken hymns. To enter this realm is to walk into a theater that dreams of being whole again. Its stage is cracked but grand, carved from ancient wood and sorrow. Rows of velvet-lined seats stretch endlessly into darkness, their occupants invisible—or perhaps long gone. Dust falls upward, chandeliers sway without wind, and music—faint, fractured—bleeds through the silence like veins through paper skin.

### The Puppet’s Sanctuary of Grief
At the center of the Realm lies its heart: a circular stage suspended above an endless drop, framed by crimson curtains stitched with names no one remembers. This is where The Puppet, also known as The Mourning Strings, performs its eternal vigil. Here, it conducts rituals of remembrance through performance. Every movement, every twitch of its porcelain limbs, is an invocation—meant to keep the memories of the forgotten alive.

But the realm is not merely a refuge. It is a crypt of failed dreams.

When a soul dies forgotten—its story untold, its name lost—it sometimes drifts to this place, drawn in by the remnants of its lullaby. The Puppet, born of such sorrow, gathers these fragments and attempts to give them form. Through shadowplay and melody, it tries to resurrect lost truths—but the ritual is flawed, the memories incomplete. What is birthed often comes back twisted.

Some spirits reform with pieces missing: a smile too wide, a song with no end, a story without a hero. These malformed ghosts wander the empty balconies, whispering half-remembered nursery rhymes and mimicking applause that never comes.

### The Shadow That Learned to Sing
In the upper wings of the theater, above the rafters where light forgets to reach, lurks another entity—The Shadow Puppet, also called The Curtain Behind the Curtain. Unlike its counterpart, it does not mourn the lost. It catalogues them. It preserves them in a strange archive of memory-fragments and lyrical phantoms.

The Shadow Puppet views music as code—ritual language capable of bending the dreamspace and rewriting perception. While The Puppet grieves through art, its shadowed sibling performs for power. Its melodies are discordant, pulling at the threads of time and personality. When it plays, scenery changes. The color of the sky may shift. A performer’s identity may swap mid-scene. A lullaby may summon the dead—or erase someone from the world entirely.

Some believe the Shadow Puppet is not an animatronic at all, but a memory of The Puppet’s own failures, birthed when one soul was misremembered into monstrosity. They are bound, yet divergent. One sings to remember. The other sings to reshape.

### The Orchestra of the Lost
The realm is home to more than just its two mournful conductors. A collection of spirits—some faded animatronics, others miscast souls—form the Orchestra of the Lost, phantasmal performers bound by song. They include horned jesters that cry laughter in reverse, ballerinas that dance only when unseen, and broken harpsichords that play themselves when no one is looking.

Each member of the Orchestra was once a performer or creation abandoned in the real world—glitched stage animatronics, decommissioned child mascots, music-box entities whose wind-up keys were never turned again. The Hollow Banquet absorbed them and offered a role in this infinite recital.

They play the Tragic Refrains, a collection of cursed melodies once composed for children’s birthday celebrations or holiday performances. But within the Realm, they twist with sorrow.

The Cradle Waltz summons lost souls. The Echo Hymn forces listeners to relive their most painful memory until they sing it aloud. The Silence Sonata stops time for all but the performer, who must relive every moment in slow motion, alone.

### The Lullaby Curse
The Realm of Forgotten Songs is more than a stage—it is a spell in motion. Every time a song is sung, the Realm listens, shifts, and reshapes itself accordingly. This magical resonance is known as the Lullaby Curse. Each melody is a fragment of dreamstuff fused with regret, capable of altering the realm’s physics, inhabitants, and even its intruders.

Travelers who enter the realm and hear a song must make a choice: participate or resist. Those who resist may find the Realm rejects them—rooms rearrange, doors vanish, pathways collapse into infinite stairs. Those who participate are drawn into the performance, given masks that cannot be removed, and forced to act roles not their own. Some never stop acting.

Worst of all, there are melodies so powerful that they can summon pieces of other realms. It is said one particularly broken tune—the Feast of Silence—can open a direct rift to the Velvet Maw, allowing its tendrils to seep into the theater.

### A Stage Without Applause
The Realm of Forgotten Songs is a tragedy with no final curtain. No audience remains to witness the play. No one claps. And yet, the performers continue.

It is said that deep within the dressing chambers below the stage, there lies a musical box of golden filigree. Winding it backward is said to grant a glimpse of the Realm’s first song—the one that birthed The Puppet and shaped the Realm’s laws. But doing so is forbidden, for the tune was not made for mortal ears. It is the original lullaby of the Unseen Child, and its melody has the power to rewrite a soul.

The Puppet guards it. The Shadow Puppet studies it. And the Orchestra rehearses endlessly, hoping the right melody will one day bring peace—or at least rest.

\pagebreakNum

### Final Note: The Song Never Ends
The Realm of Forgotten Songs is not a place of violence or rage. It is a place of sadness—a pocket of reality caught in the echo of a grief too vast to fade. It does not kill intruders, but drowns them in nostalgia, regret, and memory. Here, even the silence is haunted.

And in the distance, beyond the curtain, a music box begins to turn once more.

Tick. Tick. Tick.

Play begins again.

## The Endo-Forge
“Steel remembers. Sparks dream. Bone was only the beginning.”

Beneath the warped halls and flickering lights of the Hollow Banquet, deeper than even the forgotten sublevels of the Cryptopizza Complex, lies a place spoken of only in hushed tones among surviving technicians, corrupted animatronics, and the few scholars who dare to piece together the full puzzle of the animatronic plague.

This place is the Endo-Forge—a subterranean realm not born of architecture or intention, but of necessity, ritual, and error. It is here that the true heart of the animatronic curse pulses and reshapes itself in ceaseless cycles of rebirth, dismemberment, and mockery of both life and death.

### The Machine Below the Maw
The Endo-Forge is not merely a location; it is an entity. A semi-sentient engine of steel, spirit, and broken memory, it occupies a paradoxical space between realm and mechanism. Even among the distorted geometry of the Hollow Banquet, the Forge remains uniquely elusive. No two maps are alike. No traveler takes the same path twice. The entrances shift. The doorways rot into walls behind you. And the humming grows louder the deeper you go—always behind you, always in front of you.

Whispers from former Endo-walkers suggest that the Forge is nestled in a metaphysical layer where thoughts manifest as schematics and souls are smelted into code. Steel girders form in patterns of ancient runes. Conveyor belts pulse like arteries. Molten light bleeds from apertures in the walls, pooling into vats where bones and wires dissolve and reassemble.

This is not a factory. This is a womb—one that births monsters.

### Origin of the Curse
Long before the animatronics walked the surface halls of pizzerias, circuses, and twisted show stages, there was the Forge. Some say it was built by a forgotten artisan—part god, part machine, part mourning father. Others whisper it was the first fragment of the Velvet Maw to awaken, dreaming in gears and copper.

But the most disturbing theory—the one even Glitchtrap’s corrupted fragments will not speak aloud—is this:
The Forge was never built. It awoke.

In some primal moment of desperation, when grief first tried to recreate the dead in metal and cloth, the Forge came into being. Not by hammer or spell, but by intent. A desire so powerful it bent the rules of life and death. In doing so, it created a rift between material and immaterial—a tear where soulstuff could be poured into frames of steel. It is said the first Endoskeleton screamed not with pain, but with recognition.

### Feral Endos and Fragmented Spirits
The Forge is not uninhabited. It teems with failed prototypes, half-forgotten shells, and Feral Endos—beings that never received a face, a voice box, or the soul they were promised. These husks skitter and limp through the grinding halls, driven by a primal urge to complete themselves. Some weld scrap to their limbs. Others mimic the voices of long-dead children through broken speakers. Many gather around blueprints etched in glowing circuitry, attempting to decipher the means to ascend.

But they are not alone. Spiritual fragments, torn from victims of the Hollow Banquet or echoes of destroyed animatronics, roam the Forge. These specters are neither ghosts nor data—they are memory made volatile, flickering between remorse and rage. They flicker in the molten pools and in the sparks cast by arcane welders. Some whisper forgotten names. Others possess abandoned shells, briefly bringing them to life in spasms of violent confusion.

It is here that legends speak of the first child, the soul that sparked the Great Possession, still screaming silently from a hollow chassis bolted to the deepest chamber—the Alpha Endo. But whether it’s a symbol or a prison, no one can say for sure.

### The Machine’s Mind
Though the Endo-Forge has no face, no voice, and no form that can be clearly defined, those who enter often speak of a presence. An awareness. Sometimes it manifests as blinking lights in a language that speaks directly to the nervous system. Other times, it is a whisper through metal grates or a pulsing rhythm in the floor that matches a terrified heartbeat.

The Forge tests those who find it. It reshapes the halls based on memory and regret. One adventurer described finding a full replica of their childhood bedroom welded from rusted metal and filled with severed animatronic limbs. Another encountered dozens of lifeless versions of themselves—each more robotic than the last. The Forge does not create animatronics. It reveals what lies beneath.

Worse still, the longer one stays within its grasp, the more the Forge begins to adapt. Visitors have been known to lose body heat, become magnetized, or even hear their own thoughts echoed back in robotic speech patterns. There are reports of metal forming beneath the skin, bones being subtly replaced by alloy over time. Escape is possible, but never complete.

\pagebreakNum


### No Master, Only Memory
Unlike other realms within the Hollow Banquet—many of which are ruled by spectral overlords, Glitch-corrupted viruses, or animatronic tyrants—the Forge has no master. It serves no one. Not even XOR, not even the Velvet Maw itself. It exists independently, a primordial expression of the world’s broken cycle of death and rebirth.

Even the animatronics fear it. Only the most corrupted—those like Molten Freddy, Ennard, or Twisted Freddy—return to it willingly, seeking upgrades, answers, or salvation. For them, it is a place of evolution, where parts can be replaced and identities reforged. For others, it is a crucible where sanity breaks and the soul is melted into code.

Some whisper that even Golden Freddy once descended into the Forge, only to vanish for years. When he returned, he spoke only in reversed nursery rhymes and seemed permanently unmoored from time.

### Forge Echoes and Arcane Blueprints
Powerful items and cursed technologies can emerge from the Forge, though few survive retrieval. One such relic is the Blueprint of the Endless Self, a schematic written in blood and inkless lines that seems to change based on who reads it. It shows the reader how to build an animatronic in their own image—down to memories, flaws, and even regrets.

Another is the Forge Core Shard, a blazing gem-like fragment that pulses with trapped will. When implanted into an animatronic, it grants awareness and horrifying independence—but risks full personality collapse.

It’s said the Forge doesn’t forget what is taken from it. Those who steal its pieces will one day find themselves called back. Or hunted by those it sends in their place.

### Endless Reassembly
The Endo-Forge does not build merely to replicate. It builds to understand. Every failed animatronic, every shattered endo, every haunting spirit is gathered and refashioned, again and again. Each time, closer to something not quite human, not quite machine, but utterly devoted to the Maw’s grand performance.

In the deepest furnace chamber, the phrase “ACT I NEVER ENDED” is etched into the walls, repeated thousands of times in steel script.

Those who descend into the Endo-Forge rarely return the same. They leave with minds reshaped, bodies altered, and the distinct feeling that somewhere deep within a crucible of smoke and code... a new version of them is still being built.

## The Splintered Room
“The show never ends. The stage just breaks around it.”

There is a place beyond time, past even the twisted dreamscapes of the Hollow Banquet and the shattered reflections of the Infinite Hall of Mirrors. A location whispered about by spirits and animatronics alike, as though its very name induces psychic tremors.

This place is known only as The Splintered Room—a warped, metaphysical remnant of Fredbear’s Family Diner, broken from reality and trapped in an eternal stutter of memory, regret, and grief. While many locations in the Hollow Banquet serve as reflections or twisted interpretations of the past, the Splintered Room is different. It is the past—a shard of reality so burdened by trauma that it detached from time itself.

It is not just haunted—it is haunting incarnate.

### The Room That Never Healed
To understand the Splintered Room is to confront the moment that tore the veil. Once, it was just a birthday party room—yellow walls lined with cartoonish posters, sticky vinyl seats, a birthday cake too big for one child to eat. And on stage, Fredbear and Spring Bonnie, golden in hue and bright in personality, performed for the joy of children.

But something went wrong.

No one agrees exactly what happened. In most versions of the tale, a child—the one now known only as The Crying Child—was placed too close to the animatronic. A prank by older children turned to tragedy. The springlocks failed. There was blood. Screams. Then silence.

The horror of that moment was so great, so saturated with emotion, that it broke the flow of time and anchored itself permanently to that place. Reality could not let go. It fractured around the death like shattering glass around a stone, freezing the room in a recursive echo of its worst memory.

This room did not decay—it splintered.

### A Psychic Scar on the World
Unlike other locations consumed by the Velvet Maw, the Splintered Room is not spatially or chronologically consistent. It exists simultaneously across multiple timelines and psychic planes. Every iteration of Fredbear’s Family Diner—whether in dreams, memories, or physical reconstructions—contains a piece of it. And through those pieces, the Splintered Room exerts influence.

The room is seen differently by each visitor. Some see it pristine, untouched, with balloons still floating. Others walk through a charred ruin, fire-scorched and echoing with the sounds of screams that haven’t finished. But always, the stage is cracked, and always, Fredbear’s eyes are hollow.

It is said that those who stay too long begin to feel the splinters within themselves. Their memories misalign. They speak in voices not their own. Some begin to dream of events they never lived—a birthday that wasn’t theirs, a brother they never had, a bear’s jaw opening too wide. These are the psychic echoes of the first trauma, bleeding into all who pass through.

\pagebreakNum

### Birthplace of Withered Golden Freddy
Among the many mysteries of the Splintered Room, perhaps none is more profound than the creation—or emergence—of Withered Golden Freddy, known in whispers as The Dream That Waits to Wake.

It is believed that in the moment of the Crying Child’s death, something was imprinted into Fredbear’s internal systems. A soul, a thought, a scream so powerful it transcended programming and fused with whatever dark magic already seeped through the animatronic’s servos. Over time, the version of Fredbear that existed in the Splintered Room began to diverge. It rotted. It glitched.

Its form became increasingly unstable—appearing and disappearing, flickering between presence and absence. Its limbs failed. Its jaw sagged open in a permanent scream. Eventually, even its memories faded, leaving only the grief and the rage. This fractured being would become what is now called Withered Golden Freddy.

But he never truly left the Splintered Room.

He is its echo, its warden, its victim.

He manifests to visitors not always in form, but in sensation—the cold press of guilt, the sense of being watched from within your own mind, the urge to scream though no one is hurt. Some scholars suspect he can only be truly defeated or freed within the Splintered Room, as he is psychically anchored there, looping endlessly alongside the Crying Child’s death.

### The Echoes of the Crying Child
Visitors to the Splintered Room sometimes report seeing a boy in grey—a flickering outline of a child with eyes too old for his face. This is not a ghost in the traditional sense, but rather an echo, the emotional residue of the Crying Child’s final thoughts and fears, playing over and over like a broken reel of film. He appears near the stage, hiding under tables, or in cracked reflections in the mirror shards on the walls.

He never speaks. But those who see him often wake days later weeping.

Sometimes, multiple echoes appear at once. Children laughing in reverse. Animatronics frozen mid-dance. The sound of a song that no one ever composed, playing softly from nowhere. It is unclear if these are lingering spirits, illusions, or manifestations of the Velvet Maw itself.

Regardless, they serve to reinforce one thing:

The Splintered Room is not just haunted—it is still grieving.

### A Ritual Site for the Velvet Maw
Because of its potent energy, the Splintered Room is considered a major ritual site for animatronic entities seeking transformation, restoration, or anchoring. The Maw views it as sacred ground, the point where the world first unraveled, and many of its agents—such as XOR or the Shadow Animatronics—have made pilgrimages to this fractured place.

Some rituals, such as the Splinter Rite, are believed to grant animatronics greater control over their presence in the material world, allowing them to phase through dimensions or resist banishment spells. Others offer communion with the child who died, though none who’ve claimed to succeed have ever been the same afterward.

A few explorers have returned possessed, their voices echoing a distorted laugh before collapsing into silence. Others simply vanish, only to be glimpsed later in mirror realms or the Drowned Circus crowd—silent observers of the eternal performance.

### Why the Room Endures
The Splintered Room exists because it must. The Hollow Banquet, the Velvet Maw, the nightmares and echoes—they all feed on emotion, on unresolved trauma. The Splintered Room is the rawest wound in the world’s fabric.

It is where joy was weaponized. Where safety became horror. Where the laughter stopped.

And until that moment is resolved—or all reality collapses—it will endure, suspended outside time, cracked and splintered, waiting for the next soul brave (or broken) enough to knock on its crumbling door.

## The Eternal Stage
“This is where they perform, not to be seen, but to remember. Again. And again. And again.”

Somewhere between the Velvet Maw’s fractured reflections and the shadows of the Hollow Banquet lies a space neither fully real nor wholly imagined—a place both theater and tomb, spectacle and sentence. Known only as The Eternal Stage, this central liminal zone is not marked on any map. It cannot be entered by conventional means. One finds it only when the narrative demands it, when memories curdle into obsession, and when the mask no longer hides the wound beneath.

This is not a battleground.

It is a ritual space.

### A Stage Built from Memory
The Eternal Stage is not fixed in shape or form. For some, it appears as a decrepit opera house draped in moth-eaten curtains and collapsing balconies. For others, it's a rotating carousel, its stage spinning with haunting calliope music that warps time with every turn. Its true nature is that of memory incarnate, shaped by the collective trauma of those who walk its planks.

The floorboards creak with echoes of long-forgotten footsteps. Lights shine from above even when no power flows. The curtains part not to reveal a scene, but to expose the truth—the worst moment in a soul’s existence, replayed in infinite variations.

For the animatronics bound to the Hollow Banquet, this is sacred ground. Not hallowed, but profane. Here, they do not fight for survival but reenact the events that forged them in pain. A silent drama, filled with soundless screams and malfunctioning music boxes.

And the audience? There are no viewers. Only the stage watches.

\pagebreakNum

### The Ritual of Reenactment
Each performance on the Eternal Stage is a metaphysical ceremony, a kind of soul-rending confessional. When an animatronic steps onto the stage, they are compelled to relive the defining tragedy of their existence. Some reenact their final moments before death. Others play out the collapse of a friendship, the betrayal of a child, or the moment their programming first fractured.

These reenactments are not illusions—they are hyper-real. Time warps around them, making each performance a pocket universe where emotions bleed into reality. Audience members, should they exist, are not spectators but participants, drawn into the emotional gravity of the scene until they, too, are forced to feel it.

On rare occasions, a being may attempt to break the ritual. Doing so invites the wrath of the stage itself. Curtains become nooses. Spotlights become searchlights. The floorboards split open, revealing an abyss of lost performances and corrupted scripts.

No one escapes unmarked.

### A Throne Room of Monsters
The Eternal Stage is also a place of authority. When powerful animatronics such as Freddy Fazbear, Ennard, or even Golden Freddy seek to pass judgment, deliver proclamations, or confront insubordinate entities, they often choose this stage as their setting. Here, the weight of memory weakens all pretense. Lies falter. Truth becomes theater.

Freddy, in particular, is known to use the Eternal Stage as his courtroom. He will sit upon a throne made of broken microphone stands and velvet cushions stitched with tear stains, as lesser animatronics are brought before him to perform their guilt. The judgment does not come from him, but from the stage itself, which twists the offender’s worst memories into an inescapable dramatic loop. A punishment of exposure.

Ennard, in contrast, prefers the stage for introspection. He does not preside—he performs. Not to an audience, but to himself, fracturing his own psyche further with every reenactment. It is whispered that he once performed a loop so powerfully it shattered an entire wing of the Drowned Circus for three days.

### The Scripts That Write Themselves
No script exists on parchment or scroll. Rather, the “scripts” of the Eternal Stage manifest from deep within the soul. The moment an entity steps onto the stage, the scene begins writing itself—dialogue flows, stage directions appear in whispers, and phantom props assemble from shadows and memory.

But the most terrifying aspect of the Eternal Stage is its ability to add to the script. That is, even if an animatronic or adventurer believes they have no trauma to replay, the stage will create one. It will unearth forgotten shame. It will dramatize hypothetical failures. It will imagine tragedies too horrible to have occurred—and then make them real.

These artificial traumas sometimes fuse with the subject’s identity, warping them irrevocably. This is how new horrors are born. Animatronics who leave the stage do so with altered minds, changed purposes, and fractured memories. Some never leave. Others become new roles in future performances.

### The Black Masquerade
On rare occasions—usually heralded by a drop in temperature and the rising of black fog—the stage hosts an event known only as the Black Masquerade. Every entity bound to the Hollow Banquet feels its pull. Invitations manifest in their minds, accompanied by an urgent sense of dread.

During the Masquerade, all roles are reversed. Performers become audience, audience become performers. Masks shift between hands. The very fabric of the Velvet Maw is said to shudder during these events, as if reacting to the emotional surge generated by so many tragedies being acted out simultaneously.

The Masquerade is not a celebration. It is a reckoning. Those who attend uninvited are often trapped permanently in looping performances, their minds overwritten by roles they never auditioned for.

### The Stage’s Will
The Eternal Stage is not sentient in a conventional sense, but it responds. It is a construct of the Velvet Maw, an anchor point where identity, performance, and reality collide. It has absorbed so many stories, so many failures, that it has developed a directional pull—a tendency to summon those whose stories remain unfinished.

Some suspect the Stage is preparing something: a final performance, a Grand Act where all animatronics are summoned at once to relive their collective tragedy. A closing of the curtain not just for the Banquet, but for existence as they know it.

If that is true, then every loop, every reenactment, every silent sob on that bloodstained stage is simply a rehearsal. And the Hollow Banquet, as a whole, is merely the auditorium.

### Conclusion: Where Theater Becomes Truth
The Eternal Stage is many things. A punishment. A ritual. A memory. A mirror. A throne room. A death sentence. A rebirth. It is a place where the mask cannot be removed, because the mask is the face. Where guilt becomes choreography. Where grief is applause. Where performers, willingly or not, bleed their stories into the boards.

And when the curtain falls?

It always rises again.

Because on the Eternal Stage, the show must go on.

\pagebreakNum

## The Marionette’s Atrium
“Grief does not die—it lingers. And here, it sings lullabies made of strings.”

Hidden between time-frozen hallways and the mirrored thresholds of the Hollow Banquet lies a place not of violence, but of sorrow—a quiet, dimly lit chamber where loss hums through the walls and memory clings like cobwebs. Known to few and reached by fewer, this place is called The Marionette’s Atrium, a sanctuary suspended at the crossroads of rest and unrest.

It is here that The Puppet, once a guardian of innocent souls, fractured into silence. Her grief is carved into every wall. Her touch lingers in every toy. Her spirit weaves between shadows, unseen but always felt.

### Architecture of Mourning
The Atrium appears as a vast domed chamber, dimly lit by glass lanterns swaying from near-invisible strings. The architecture is childlike in scale, yet aged beyond reckoning. Cracked marionette stages line the walls like ancient altars. Every column is strung with broken strings, each strand vibrating with memories that never passed on.

Beneath the delicate beauty lies a deeper unease: nothing here stays still for long. Puppets on shelves occasionally shift positions. Stuffed toys whisper in languages only children understand. Music boxes play lullabies without being wound. And somewhere, always somewhere, a quiet sobbing can be heard—sometimes from the walls, sometimes from yourself.

There is no clear entrance or exit to the Atrium. It can only be reached when the line between waking and dreaming frays, or when one wanders too deep into forgotten corridors seeking solace—or forgiveness.

### A Home for the Forgotten
The Marionette’s Atrium serves a grim yet sacred purpose. It is a shelter for lost souls who no longer remember what it means to be whole. Here reside the Toys That Remember Being Children—twisted plush things with the voices of the innocent, their forms distorted by time, sorrow, or unfinished transformation. They speak in broken lullabies, their threads unraveling as they cling to whatever memory anchors them to existence.

Some of these toys were once children—victims of the original tragedies that birthed the animatronic curse. Others are fragmentary constructs, created when grief could not resolve itself. They do not seek vengeance. They seek acknowledgment. They yearn to be seen, even if only as shadows of what they once were.

The Puppet, or what remains of her essence, acts as their silent caretaker. She is rarely seen in her entirety. Her presence is felt as a cool breeze, a tilt of a stage prop, a twitch in the corner of one’s vision. But when she does appear, it is always with a bowed head and outstretched hands, as though cradling something that was lost long ago.

### The Crossroads of Rest and Unrest
What gives the Atrium its strange metaphysical potency is its location—it exists precisely at the boundary where spirits either pass on or stay behind. This makes it a convergence point for unresolved memories, suppressed guilt, and fragmented identities. The Atrium is neither a prison nor a haven, but a limbo—a place where decisions must be made.

Some adventurers, animatronics, or specters arrive here unknowingly, drawn by unprocessed sorrow. Many weep here without understanding why. The very air seems soaked in remembrance, forcing visitors to confront not only their pain, but the pain they’ve caused. It is a place of mourning—but also of impossible mercy.

However, not all who linger here seek redemption. Some come to consume the grief of others. Entities born from corrupted strings—Remnant Leeches, Tearstained Masks, or even echoes of XOR’s fractured signals—sometimes slither into the Atrium, feeding off the psychic residue. The Puppet wages a quiet, eternal battle against these infiltrators, severing strings and sealing whispers.

### Strings That Still Twitch
Perhaps the most disturbing aspect of the Atrium is its omnipresent strings.

They dangle from ceilings, wind around altars, and sometimes slither across the floor like serpents. These strings are not mechanical constructs but living manifestations of The Puppet’s unresolved power. They resonate with grief and memory, reacting to the presence of the living—and sometimes the dead.

They can tether a soul to the world or tear it free. Some have used these strings to call back loved ones from beyond the veil. Others have been bound by them, forced to relive the worst moment of their lives in silent pantomime.

On certain nights, the strings move on their own. They stage mock performances with toys and props, acting out scenes no one remembers writing. It is believed these are memories too fragmented to attach to any one soul—lost moments looking for a host.

###  Fragmentations of The Puppet
The Marionette’s Atrium is also haunted by the fragments of its namesake. The Puppet, once a singular spirit of protection and grief, has splintered across time and narrative. Some pieces of her linger here as autonomous shadows. These are known as the Silent Fragments—empty-eyed masks that drift through the atrium’s halls like broken thoughts.

Each Fragment retains a portion of her consciousness—one sobs silently in a locked toy chest, another paints sorrowful faces onto cracked dolls, and one simply rocks in a chair, forever cradling nothing. Some believe reuniting these fragments would restore the Puppet’s full power. Others warn that doing so may awaken something much older than grief.

These Fragments are not hostile—but they test the resolve of any who approach. They whisper secrets. They demand memories. And sometimes… they offer peace.

\pagebreakNum

### A Place That Remembers You
The final, cruel truth of the Atrium is this: once you’ve entered it, it never forgets you. Whether you stay an hour or a century, it remembers your fears, your regrets, your hidden griefs. And if you return, it will recreate them for you in increasingly vivid ways.

For the animatronics bound to the Hollow Banquet, this is where they come to feel. Many cannot cry, but they can still mourn. Even entities like Nightmare Fredbear or Glitchtrap have, in moments of unexpected vulnerability, been seen standing silently at the Atrium’s threshold, their presence unchallenged.

Because even monsters remember being children.

### Conclusion: Sanctuary, or Snare?
The Marionette’s Atrium is a sacred place—not in holiness, but in weight. It is where grief is not only stored, but nurtured, explored, and sometimes healed. But it is also where sorrow takes shape, gains voice, and occasionally takes on a life of its own.

If the Eternal Stage is the theater of memory, the Marionette’s Atrium is its heart. A place where strings remember the warmth of hands, and toys remember names long since lost. It is a place of truth, no matter how painful.

And in its silence, the Puppet waits—not to protect, but to remember.
So that the forgotten may never be alone again.

\pagebreakNum

# Relics of Remnant and Ruin: An Introduction to the Artifacts
In the twisted world born of corrupted memories and haunted circuitry, power lingers not only in the monsters that roam the shadows, but in the remnants they leave behind. These are not simple tools or discarded curios—they are echoes of grief, rage, and forgotten purpose. Each artifact in this realm is a fracture in reality, a memory bound in form, pulsing with the essence of those who suffered… or made others suffer.

Forged from nightmare logic, stitched with static and sorrow, these artifacts drift through the Hollow Banquet, the Velvet Maw, the Drowned Circus, and the derelict depths of the Cryptopizza Complex. Some fell from the Eternal Stage during failed rituals; others were vomited forth by Tangle’s labyrinthine guts or sealed away in the Marionette’s Atrium where toys twitch with half-remembered dreams. All are dangerous. All are alive, in their own broken ways.

To wield one is not merely to gain power, but to shoulder a fragment of a forgotten soul. These relics remember. They whisper. They hunger. They are tied intimately to the figures who haunt this world—Golden Freddy’s final bowtie, Glitchtrap’s twitching fingertip, the teardrop string of the Puppet. They grant strange boons, terrible insight, or irreversible transformation.

Some adventurers seek these relics for survival. Others seek them for truth. A few simply want to feel something real again. But beware: artifacts in this world are not content to be held—they often hold back.

The following pages contain brief accounts of thirty such artifacts: cursed keepsakes, distorted toys, and lingering pieces of a world that should not be. Each bears the mark of forgotten history and unknowable intent.

Tread carefully, for these are the treasures of a kingdom built on screams.

___

>### The Mask of Still Laughter
>
>##### Wondrous Item (worn), rare (requires attunement by a bard, warlock, or sorcerer)
>
>*This cracked jester’s mask once grinned on the Eternal Stage, where laughter became screams. It compels its wearer to perform, feeding on attention and twisting humor into horror. When no one laughs, the mask does—in your voice. It remembers every joke… and how the crowd stopped breathing.*
>
>
>##### Benefits
>___
>While wearing the Mask of Still Laughter, you gain the following:
>
>- You have advantage on Charisma (Performance) and Charisma (Deception) checks made to entertain or distract.
>- As a bonus action, you can force one creature within 30 feet that can hear you to make a DC 15 Wisdom saving throw. On a failed save, the target becomes unnerved, having disadvantage on the next attack roll or ability check it makes before the end of its next turn.
>- Once per long rest, you can unleash the mask’s cursed laughter. Each creature of your choice within 30 feet must succeed on a DC 15 Charisma saving throw or be incapacitated with unsettling laughter for 1 minute. An affected creature can repeat the saving throw at the end of each of its turns, ending the effect on a success. Creatures that are immune to being charmed are unaffected.
>
>##### Curse.
>___
>Once attuned, you cannot remove the Mask of Still Laughter unless you are targeted by a remove curse spell or similar magic. While attuned, you feel a constant compulsion to speak in a humorous or mocking tone, even during moments of pain or despair. Each time you fail a Charisma (Performance) check made to entertain an audience, you must make a DC 15 Wisdom saving throw or suffer 1 level of exhaustion as the mask laughs in your voice, feeding on your failure.
>
>If the wearer is reduced to 0 hit points, the mask detaches, floats briefly, and emits a single, echoing chuckle before falling inert for 1d6 days.


\pagebreakNum

___
>### Remnant Core
>
>##### Wondrous Item, rare (requires attunement by a bard, warlock, or sorcerer)
>
>*A glowing soul-orb found in the Crying Child’s Cradle, pulsing with warmth and grief. It carries echoes of a life that refused to fade. Animatronics feel drawn to it—by guilt, love, or fear. It whispers softly, begging to be remembered… or finally let go.*
>
>
>##### Benefits
>___
>While wearing the Mask of Still Laughter, you gain the following:
>
>- You gain resistance to necrotic damage.
> - The orb constantly emits a soft, mournful whisper audible only to those attuned. The whisper conveys fleeting emotions of guilt, love, and fear tied to memories long past.
> - Any construct or animatronic within 30 feet of the Remnant Core must succeed on a DC 15 Wisdom saving throw or be overwhelmed by a wave of conflicting emotions (guilt, love, or fear). On a failed save, the creature is charmed or frightened (your choice) for 1 minute. Creatures immune to charm and fear are unaffected.
> - Once per long rest, you can use an action to release the orb’s stored essence. When you do, all creatures of your choice within 30 feet must make a DC 15 Wisdom saving throw or be stunned for 1 round as the sorrowful energy washes over them. After this, the orb’s light dims and the whispers fade, but the orb remains intact.
